==PAGE:3==
Abstract
Cross-laminated timber (CLT) has gained significant popularity as a construction mate-
rial since the early 2000s in many countries worldwide. The advantages of building with
CLT are many, however, acoustically the material accommodates challenges.
These
challenges are being faced with numerous laboratory tests on sound insulation of CLT-
elements with various build-ups and configurations. However, specified requirements for
CLT-elements are lacking in the ISO standard series 10140 1:5, leaving room for inter-
pretation by individual test facilitators and laboratories regarding how these sound mea-
surements are conducted. This contributes to increased variation in measurement results
between acoustic laboratories and gives rise to uncertainty around the trustwhorthyness
of the results.
This thesis study investigates the test procedure and the test arrangement for conducting
laboratory sound insulation measurements on CLT floor elements. The main focus area
restricts to investigations of mounting conditions of the floor in the laboratory, and secondly
the repeatability and reliability of low frequency measurements. From sound insulation
measurements and structural reverberation time measurements on numerous plausible
laboratory mountings, the study reveals that loading the floor with 25 kg/m2 can reduce the
variance in measurement results, and that rigidly fixating the floor in the test opening can
reduce it even further. The reliability and repeatability of low frequency measurements
were only briefly studied due to time restrictions, but indicated however, that even with an
increasing number of microphone positions, the uncertainty in the low-frequency range
remained relatively high.
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements

Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
vii
==PAGE:8==
1
Introduction
This thesis study investigates the structural building component - CLT, and is specifically
focusing on the CLT floor and the lack of standardised sound insulation measurement
procedure for these floors in laboratories.
1.1
Background Information
CLT stands for cross-laminated timber, and was firstly patented in 1923 by the two Amer-
icans; Frank J. Wars and Robert Watts. Back then the material wasn’t used much in the
construction industry, but from the early 90’s it gained traction in Germany and Austria
for residential and commercial properties, and today it is widely used in Europe, North
America and Australia in residential and commercial buildings [1]. It is still increasing in
popularity due to its sustainable qualities, its lightness, structural strength and its archi-
tectural appeal.
CLT is a construction material assembly that consists of solid wood lamellas staggered
in 90-degree crosswise layers and glued together with structural adhesive [2]. It comes
in many variants with varying number of layers, layer thicknesses and different wood
species, all depending on its purpose of use and on the geographical location. A min-
imum of three layers is required to achieve the necessary strength; however, five-layer
assemblies are most commonly used and the floor densities typically range from 450-550
kg/m3 [2].
1.2
Motivation
While CLT floors are widely used in buildings and tested in laboratories, standardized
measurement procedures for laboratory sound insulation measurements on floors lack
specific descriptions and specified requirements for CLT floors. Such descriptions and
requirements are necessary as CLT floors are solid, lightweight structures, that thereby
doesn’t fall into the categories of any of the reference floors in the ISO standard series
10140 1:5 [3, 4, 5, 6, 7]. Therefore, there is a clear need for continued work with the stan-
dards so that manufactures and the consultants gains a common ground for competition,
marketing and development.
Earlier studies have showed that the CLT floors are mounted differently globally depending
on the laboratory at which the measurements are performed [2]. Since the impact of
different mountings on sound insulation measurement results has not been previously
investigated, this thesis was specifically motivated to focus on mounting conditions.
Low-frequency measurements have long been debated among acousticians regarding
their reliability. Today, some consistently measure and report sound insulation down to
20 Hz to allow for an idea of the test element’s behaviour. Others only measure down
to the required 100 Hz to avoid potentially misleading or inaccurate data. For lightweight
construction materials, like CLT, the low frequencies seems to be a larger issue, and
therefore investigations of low frequency measurements will be made in this thesis.
1.3
Project Formulation
This thesis study focuses on the test procedure and the test arrangement for conducting
laboratory sound insulation measurements on CLT floor elements. The main focus area of
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
1
==PAGE:9==
this thesis will restrict to investigations of mounting conditions of the floor in the laboratory,
and secondly the repeatability and reliability of low frequency measurements.
The thesis study aims at answering how different mounting conditions of a CLT floor in
the laboratory affect sound insulation measurement results and preferably come up with
suggestions to mounting requirements to adopt in the standard. Additionally, this thesis
study aims to investigate the uncertainty in sound insulation measurement results in the
lower third-octave bands 50 Hz, 63 Hz and 80 Hz. All measurements were conducted in
the acoustic reverberation chambers 004 and 904 at DTU Lyngby Campus.
1.4
Framework for Investigating Topics
This section is included to give the reader an overview of how the addressed topics of this
thesis have been investigated. Many mountings have been tested and many analyses
have been made, both during and after measurements, which may make it challenging
for a first-time reader to comprehend and piece everything together.
The investigated mountings were selected based on three criteria: those currently used in
laboratories worldwide [2, Interviews], those resembling in-situ mounting practices [2], and
those previously used for other floor types in the DTU laboratory. Furthermore, the mount-
ings were tested both with and without lead rubber covering the floor edges to enhance
airtightness. Additionally, due to the relatively low weight of the structural component,
tests were conducted both with and without loading (25 kg/m2).
Figure 1.1: Block diagram of mountings investigated in this project.
The measurement methods used in this project can be divided into two main parts: (1)
structural reverberation time and sound insulation measurements, used to analyse the
2
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:10==
effect from the specific mounting on the floor’s sound insulation properties, and (2) re-
peatability measurements, consisting of 30 sound pressure level measurements for each
of four different scenario configurations.
Figure 1.2: Block diagram of the measurement methods used in this project.
Most effort has been put into analysing the effect of the mounting of the floor on the sound
insulation measurement results. Due to time limitations and restrictions the repeatability
and reliability of sound insulation measurements in the low third-octave bands, 50 Hz, 63
Hz and 80 Hz, haven’t been analysed that thorough. However, all measurement data has
been included in the appendix, to allow curious readers to make use of the data.

2
Theory
The most relevant theory that has been used in this study is presented in this section.
Some of the theoretical concepts that lay ground for the the used measurement proce-
dures have been omitted as well as equations for basic descriptive statistics. The main
reference sources for the building acoustic theory sections are combined theory from J.H.
Rindel’s ”Sound Insulation in Buildings”, Carl Hopkins’ ”Sound Insulation” and Lecture
Note 4214 from the Architectural Acoustic course at DTU [8, 9, 10]. The signal process-
ing theory is from the Acoustic Signal Processing course at DTU and the statistics theory
is from internet webpages. Specific references will be provided where relevant.
2.1
Airborne- and impact sound
Airborne sound is longitudinal waves that propagates through air. The source putting the
air molecules into movement can be e.g. human voices or loudspeakers. The sound
waves can be either reflected, absorbed or transmitted when incident onto another sur-
face or object. The ratio of the reflected, absorbed and transmitted sound depends on
the properties of the structures that the waves encounter, and the angle of incidence. In
building acoustics, airborne sound is of interest in terms of the airborne sound insulation
properties of the building and the structural building components. Minimizing airborne
sound transmission between adjacent rooms or apartments will improve the acoustic per-
formance of the building.
To determine the acoustic performance of a separate structural building component, lab-
oratory measurements of airborne sound insulation can be conducted, and has been in
this study. The laboratory setup consists of two adjacent rooms, a sending room and a
receiving room, separated by the structural building component under test. By having
an omnidirectional loudspeaker in the sending room playing loud pink noise, the airborne
sound insulation is evaluated in terms of the sound reduction from sending- to receiving-
room. The sound reduction index is obtained by
R = L1 −L2 −10 · log10
A2
S ,
(2.1)
where R is the sound reduction index, L1 and L2 is the sound pressure level in the source
room and the receiving room, respectively, A2 is the absorption area in the receiving
room and S is the surface area of the structural building component under test. The
absorption area of the receiving room is obtained from Sabines formula from the measured
reverberation time in the receiving room;
RT = 55.3
c
· V
A,
(2.2)
where c is the speed of sound in air, i.e. 343 m/s at 20°C and atmospheric pressure, V is
the volume of the receiving room and A is the absorption area given by
A = S · ¯α,
(2.3)
where S is the total surface area of the room and ¯α is the mean absorption coefficient of
all surfaces in the room.
4
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:12==
Impact sound refers to structure-borne sound in solid structures caused by excitation from
an impact source. Examples include footsteps on a floor, dropped LEGO blocks, or a nail
hammered into a wall. As these sources illustrate, impact sound, like airborne sound, is
of great importance in building acoustics and essential for evaluating a structural com-
ponent’s sound insulation properties. Impact sound insulation is measured with a similar
setup as for airborne sound insulation measurements, and is evaluated in terms of the
normalized impact sound pressure level;
Ln = Li + 10 · log10
( A
A0
)
,
(2.4)
where Li is the sound pressure level in the receiving room, A is the equivalent absorp-
tion area and A0 = m2 is the reference equivalent absorption area. A high Ln indicates
that much of the wave energy is transmitted to the adjacent room, i.e. poor sound insula-
tion. Impact excitation typically excites lower frequencies than airborne sound excitation.
The lower frequencies often are troublesome to mitigate with sound insulation, and this
problem is especially present in lightweight buildings, like buildings of wood.
2.1.1
Critical frequency
The critical frequency is often of concern in building acoustics as this is the frequency at
and above which the bending waves in a vibrating building component couples with the
longitudinal waves in the surrounding air. Thereby, the plate will easily radiate sound. The
bending wave and the longitudinal wave couples at the angles at which their wave speeds
coincide;
cB =
cl
sin(θ),
(2.5)
where cB is the bending wave speed, cl is the longitudinal wave speed and θ is the angle
of incidence in the range of 0◦< θ < 90◦. The critical frequency is the first coincidence
frequency, at θ = 90◦, and is obtained by
fc = c2
2π
√m
B =
c2
π · lz
√
3ρm(1 −µ2)
E
,
(2.6)
where c is the speed of sound in air, B is the bending stiffness of the plate per unit width,
ρm is the density of the plate, E is Young’s Modulus, µ is Poisson’s ratio and lz is the
thickness of the plate [8]. Note that lz has the symbol h in J.H. Rindel’s book.
There will be other frequencies above the critical frequency at which the wavelength of
the longitudinal- and that of the bending wave will coincide. Therefore, it is desired to
have as high a critical frequency as possible in building structures. The thinner and the
lighter a structural building component is, the lower the critical frequency. A thicker and
heavier component will oppositely have a higher critical frequency.
2.1.2
Resonances in a plate
Resonance frequencies are also of relevance regarding sound insulation properties of
a structural building component. Resonance frequencies are frequencies at which the
dimensions of the structure matches a positive integer multiple of half the wavelength.
The resonance frequencies are associated with the normal modes in the plate. For low
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements

frequencies it is normally acceptable to assume bending waves and therefore the natural
frequencies in the plate are determined by
fmn = c2
4fc

(2.7)
where m and n are the mode number in the length-, x, and width direction, y, respectively,
and lx and ly are the length and width of the floor, respectively [10]. The natural frequen-
cies are usually well separated in the low frequencies but towards higher frequencies the
modal density, ∆N/∆f, and the modal overlap, M, increases.
From the equation it can be seen that the smaller the dimensions of the plate the higher
the first resonance frequencies. Therefore, in sound insulation measurement results it is
often not expected to be able to detect resonance frequencies occurring in the thickness
direction, z, within the frequency range of interest. However, for thick plates resonance
frequencies in the thickness direction can appear in the sound insulation measurement
results. They occur at frequencies at which the thickness of the plate is equal to a positive
integer multiple of half a wavelength in the solid material and can be determined by
fn,z = nzcz
2lz
,
(2.8)
where nz is the mode number in the z direction, cz is the wave speed in the structural
material in the z direction and lz is the thickness [11]. c has been replaced by cz in this
report, to emphasize the direction of the waves.
2.2
Theoretical transmission loss and normalised impact
sound pressure level from SEA analysis.
The theoretical transmission loss, R, and normalised impact sound pressure level, Ln, has
been studied with the aim of reaching a better understanding of the measured results. The
formulas for R and Ln are are derived from Statistical Energy Analysis (SEA) - a method
based on statistical energy transfer between coupled resonant systems. Like any other
method, SEA takes some general ground assumptions:
• Only resonant fields are of importance,
• The modal density should be high and smooth,
• There should be a more or less diffuse field,
• The coupling between different subsystems should be weak.
These assumptions require careful consideration when applying SEA.
Two resonant systems are said to be coupled if there is a physical connection between
the two systems, and if the natural frequencies of the two systems are sufficiently close
to each other. The latter may be fulfilled if the modal density within every considered
frequency band is greater than one [10]. In SEA, statistical modal densities for typical
systems has been derived, and the ones that are used in this project are provided in the
following table:
6

==PAGE:14==
Table 2.1: Statistical modal density of some typical systems considered in SEA [10].
System
Modal Density, ∆N/∆f

Thick plate, shear waves
2π
c2s
Sf
If a system has low modal density, SEA-derived formulas can still be applied by manually
counting the modes in the system to determine the actual modal density. However, this
violates the second general assumption, and will potential affect how well the theoretical
R and Ln predicted by SEA aligns with the measured results.
From Table 2.1 it is seen that the statistical modal density is different depending on the
dominating wave type in the plate. fs is named the cross-over frequency from bending
waves to shear waves. However, it has been found that the cross-over actually happens
at fs/2. Below fs/2 bending waves will dominate in the structure and above fs/2 shear
waves will dominate.
2.2.1
Loss factor
Energy transfer between systems can only happen via physical connections. Therefore,
in order to investigate the amount of energy transfer between coupled systems, it is of
relevance to study the loss factor. The loss factor is a descriptor of the damping of a
vibrating system and is defined as the relative energy loss per radian[8] - the dissipated
energy in a time period of vibration.
Considering a plate, which is the case in this study, the loss factor can be divided into
three contributions; internal losses, radiation losses and coupling losses. Combined they
constitute the total loss factor of the plate;
ηtotal = ηint + ηrad + ηborder.
(2.9)
The internal losses, ηint, describe the inherent material damping, i.e. internal losses that
converts vibrational energy of a plate into heat. The radiation losses regards the radiated
energy due to resonant vibrations in the plate. The radiation loss factor from plate to room
is
ηrad =
2ρairc
ωmsurface
· σres,
(2.10)
where σres is the radiation efficiency described later in this section. In the equation the
multiplication with 2 is due to the sound radiation from both side of the plate. The border
loss factor, ηborder, also referred to as the coupling loss factor ηcoupling [9], concerns the
boundary losses from the plate to the surrounding structure and is obtained by
ηcoupling = X
√f .
(2.11)
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
7
==PAGE:15==
It has been found that X = 1 is a reasonable estimate for field measurements of mason-
ry/concrete walls and floors. For laboratory measurements, coupling losses are smaller.
Therefore, to minimize measurement variations across different laboratories, the ISO
standard 10140-5 specifies a minimum loss factor requirement based on X > 0.3 [9].
The minimum loss factor requirement in the ISO standard for structures with a surface
mass greater than 150 kg/m2 is
ηmin = 0.01 + 0.3
√f .
(2.12)
This corresponds to X = 0.3 and ηi = 0.01 [7]. The radiation losses are typically negligibly
small, and are therefore not included in this formula [9].
Loss factor obtained from measurements
The total loss factor of a system can be obtained for rooms and for solid structures by
measuring the reverberation time and using it in the formula:
ηtotal = 2.2
fT ,
(2.13)
where T is the measured reverberation time. This is the standard measurement method
for loss factors and is described in the ISO standard 10140-4 [6].
2.2.2
Radiation efficiency for forced and resonant vibrations
When using SEA to determine the transmission loss, R, and the normalised impact sound
pressure level, Ln, for a vibrating plate coupled with large reverberation chambers on both
sides, as is the case in this study, it is necessary to determine the the plate’s radiation
efficiency.
A plate excited by an external source, will radiate sound into the surrounding medium.
In the analysis of R, where the plate is excited by incident waves from a diffuse sound
field, both forced and resonant vibrations contribute to the radiated sound. In certain fre-
quency ranges the radiated sound from forced vibrations will dominate over the radiated
sound from resonant vibrations and vice versa. The domination of either forced or res-
onant transmission holds information about the plate’s characteristics. In the analysis of
normalised impact sound pressure level, where the plate is mechanically excited by a
point source, such as the hammers from a tapping machine, the sound radiation from the
plate will be due to resonant vibrations.
The contribution from each type of vibration is determined from their respective radiation
efficiencies. The forced radiation efficiency from diffuse field excitation is determined by,
σfor,d = 1
2 ln(ke),
(2.14)
where k is the wavenumber and e is the characteristic dimension;
e = 4S
U = 2lxly
lx + ly
,
(2.15)
where U is the length of the perimeter and S is the surface area of the plate.

frequency of interest. For small thick plates where f11 > fc, the radiation efficiency follows
the graph shown in Figure 2.1.

As it can be seen the radiation efficiency for small thick plates radiates differently in dif-
ferent frequency ranges. fb is calculated as
fb = fc + 10c
U .

The radiation efficiency for thin large plates, where f11 < fc, has a different behaviour in
between f11 and fc. However, this part is not described any further in this report, as it is
known that f11 > fc for the CLT floor used in this study.

The forced transmission loss isn’t derived from SEA, but from the wall impedance method
(not described in this report). The forced transmission loss from random incidence exci-
tation is
Rf = R0 −10 log10(2σfor,d),
(2.17)

2.3
Signal Processing
Signal processing was necessary to determine the structural reverberation time of the test
element in third-octave bands. All post processing was performed in MATLAB R2024a.
The setup for structural reverberation time measurements was treated as a linear time-
invariant system, LTI, which holds many advantages in digital signal processing and greatly
simplifies the analysis. The LTI system can be modelled as in Figure 2.2.
Figure 2.2: Diagram of an LTI system.
The system is considered as a black box, H, that treats the given input, x, in a certain way
that gives the output, y,
y(t) = H{x(t)}.
(2.23)
The linearity consideration holds the concept of superposition,
H {a1x1(t) + a2x2(t)} = a1H {x1(t)} + a2H {x2(t)} ,
(2.24)
10 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:18==
where a1 and a2 is two arbitrary scalars, and from this it can be seen that only frequencies
that goes into the system, comes out. The time-invariance assumption is that the system
does not change over time,
y (t −t0) = H {x (t −t0)} .
(2.25)
The equations above are given in the time domain, but can be transferred to the frequency
domain with the Fourier transform;
X(ω) =
∫∞
−∞
x(t)e−jωt dt,
(2.26)
where ω = 2πf is the angular frequency. Equation 2.26 is written for the transient input
signal, but is also valid for the output signal, y(t), and the system response, h(t). The
inverse Fourier transform can be used to go back from the the frequency domain to the
time domain and therefore
x(t), y(t), h(t) ⇔X(ω), Y (ω), H(ω)
(2.27)
can be written as Fourier transform pairs, where H here is the impulse response of the
system in the frequency domain.
By knowing the input, i.e. the signal that is fed into the system, and measuring the output,
a complete characterization of the system’s response to an arbitrary input can be obtained,
if the the signal has a flat lined frequency spectrum. In this study, the system was excited
using Gaussian white noise, which, with increasing averaging, produces a flat frequency
spectrum. White noise was chosen over an impulse response due to its ease of repro-
duction and to avoid distortion caused by the sudden high-energy burst characteristic of
an impulse.
2.3.1
One-channel and two-channel FFT
In this project the black box was the test element. The system outlined in Figure 2.2 is
a one channel measurement, as it is only the output that is measured. The input is the
measurement signal and is thereby already known. However, for the measurement setup
in this thesis project, the impedance of the system was assumed to be of same magni-
tude as the impedance of the source. Therefore, the impact from the source wouldn’t be
negligible in the measured results, and therefore a two-channel measurement setup was
needed. This setup can be modelled as in Figure 2.3.
Figure 2.3: Bow diagram of the LTI system modelled with the source.
In this block diagram both input, x, and output, y, is measured and used in the further
digital signal processing, DSP, analysis.
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
11
==PAGE:19==
2.3.2
Coherence
The coherence is useful function that provide information about how distorted conducted
measurement was by noise. It compares the measured input signal to the measured
output signal to ensure that the frequency content, that was fed into the system is the
same as the outcome frequency content, i.e. the concept from linearity.
The coherence is
γ2
xy(ω) =
|Sxy(ω)|2
Sxx(ω)Syy(ω) = H1(ω)
H2(ω),
(2.28)
where H1(ω) and H2(ω) are frequency response estimate functions given by the auto-
and cross-spectra; Sxx, Sxy and Syy. H1(ω) is given by
H1(ω) = Sxy(ω)
Sxx(ω),
(2.29)
and H2(ω) is given by
H2(ω) = Syy(ω)
S∗xy(ω),
(2.30)
where the star,∗, is the complex conjugate. Both are frequency responses of the system,
but H1(ω) is calculated from the autospectrum of the input signal and H2(ω) from the au-
tospectrum of the output signal. Therefore, H2(ω) is more sensitive to noise and therefore
the ratio of H1(ω) to H2(ω) will reveal if the measurement was more or less noisy. The
coherence is a value between zero and one, where one reveals no noisy measurements
and zero the opposite.
2.3.3
ETC, EDC and RT60
The reverberation time of the system under test (in this study the CLT floor) are estimated
with the following post processing of the measured impulse responses.
Firstly the Energy-Time Curve, ETC, were computed by taking the square of the measured
impulse response,
ETClog(t) = 10 log10
(
h2(t)
)
,
(2.31)
where h(t) is the measured impulse response. The ETC shows the exponential decay
of the impulse response. If calculated in dB, as showed in Equation 2.31, the decay
is linear. The ETC is used to estimate the noise floor in the measurements stemming
from e.g. electrical noise or background noise in the environment, and to ensure that the
dynamic range in the measurements, i.e. the range from the measured peak value to the
noise floor, is large enough. The dynamic range must be sufficiently large to estimate the
reverberation time. At least 30 dB is required to estimate T20 in order to take the 20 dB
decay from -5 dB to at least 5 dB above the noise floor[12].
Secondly the energy decay curve, EDC, was simulated. The EDC is obtained from the
ETC, by truncating the ETC at the knee point, i.e. the point in time at which the noise floor
is reached, and then apply backward integration to smooth out the random fluctuations in
the ETC. The Schroeder backward integration executed as;
12 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:20==
EDCdB(t) = 10 log10
(∫∞
t
h2(τ)dτ
∫∞
0 h2(τ)dτ
)
= 10 log10
(
1 −
∫t
0 h2(τ)dτ
∫∞
0 h2(τ)dτ
)
,
(2.32)
where τ is a time dummy. The EDC curve will allow for a more precise characterization of
the reverberation time as the smoothening of the ETC will ensure neither to overestimate
nor to underestimate the reverberation time.
Lastly the reverberation time is estimated from the EDC. The definition of the reverberation
time, RT60, is the time it takes for the energy to decay with 60 dB. Typically in acoustics
the RT60 is based on an either 20 dB or 30 dB decay, i.e. T20 and T30, respectively. While
T30 is more sensitive to noisy environments, it also offers a more true estimate of the
reverberation time, since it reflects a wider range of the actual energy decay. However, in
this project, T20 was used due to insufficient dynamic range.
The T20 is estimated by fitting a linear curve between the -5 dB and -25 dB points on
the EDC. This linear fit is then extrapolated down to -60 dB, where the time at which
-60 dB is reached is the reverberation time, RT60 or T20. If the dynamic range in the
measurements is too small, the second point of the RT60 will fall on the end tail of the EDC
thereby underestimating the reverberation time. Conversely, if too much of the noise floor
is present in the EDC the reverberation time will be overestimated. This highlights the
importance of a good dynamic range and a proper truncation of the signal as mentioned
previously in this section.
2.3.4
Filtering
It was desired to obtain the reverberation time of the structure under test in third-octave
bands with center frequencies from 50 Hz to 5000 Hz. Therefore the truncated impulse
responses were filtered before computing the EDCs.
The filtering was done with a bandpass butterworth filter design of 3rd order. The upper
and lower frequency bounds of each third-octave band were calculated like
fu = fn

and
fl = fn · 2

(2.34)
where fu and fl is the upper and lower cut-off frequency, respectively, and fn is the center

2.4
Statistics
As stated in the beginning of this section some equations for basic descriptive and in-
ferential statistics have been omitted. However, the concepts of two sampled t-test and
standard and modified Z-scores are briefly described in the following.
2.4.1
two sampled t-test
The Student’s t-test is used to determine whether ”... the response of two groups is statis-
tically significant” [13]. The t-test can be one-sampled or two-sampled, and in this report
the two-sampled has been used. The two-sampled t-test tests for the null hypothesis,
that two data samples with normal distribution and of equal variance are from populations
with equal means. The result from the t-test will be zero or one respectively meaning
acceptance and rejection of the null hypothesis. In this thesis study the MATLAB function
ttest2, that has a default significance level of 5%, has been used [14].
2.4.2
Standard and modified Z-scores
When considering a population, x, the standard Z-score gives the number of standard
deviations that a value within that population, xi, is away from the mean of the population
[15].
Zstandard = xi −µ
σ
,
(2.35)
where µ here is the mean value of the population and σ is the standard deviation.
The standard Z-score is sensitive to outliers that can largely affect the mean value of the
population. Instead, a modified Z-score can be used, which considers the median of the
population, ˜x, and how many median absolute deviations, MAD, a value, xi, deviates from
the median [16]. It is calculated with the formula;
Zmodified = 0.6745xi −˜x
MAD ,
(2.36)
where MAD is the median absolute deviation
MAD = median(|xi −˜x|).
(2.37)
The modified Z-score is more robust to outliers. If the modified Z-score for a given data
point in the population is above 3.5 or below -3.5, the data point is labelled as a potential
outlier [17].
14 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:22==
3
Experimental Setup
In this thesis study two types of measurements were conducted; structural reverberation
time measurements and sound insulation measurements. The same CLT floor was used
throughout all measurements. The CLT floor constituted of two panels connected with
screws - a common CLT floor configuration both in-situ and in laboratories [2]. It was
desired to change as little as possible to the mounting setup between measurements and
therefore, the two types of measurements were conducted on one type of mounting before
changing to the next.
All measurements were conducted at the acoustic laboratories at DTU Lyngby Campus
between September 19, 2024, and December 28, 2024. The measurement period were
this long due to time required for coordinating lab bookings and test equipment among
multiple external parties, delays in test materials, decisions regarding the test setup design
for structural reverberation time measurements, and the concurrent completion of a DTU
course alongside this thesis project.
The following sections provide a detailed description of;
• the measurement setups,
• the preparations of the floor made prior to measurements and
• the mounting variations examined in this project.
3.1
Structural reverberation time
The design of the structural reverberation time measurement setup demanded more prepa-
ration than the setup for the sound insulation measurements. This was due to such large-
scale measurements being less common to perform than sound insulation measurements
and due to internal discussions regarding the choice of software.
The design process of the measurement setup and the decisions made to arrive at the
final measurement setup are described in detail in the following sections. Selected results
are presented with the purpose of justifying the choices made.
This section includes many references to the various mountings tested in this thesis study.
The mountings will be described in detail in Section 3.5 and it is recommended for the
reader to become familiar with the overall mountings and mounting variations before pro-
ceeding reading this section.
3.1.1
Core setup of shaker, force transducer and accelerometer
The structural reverberation time measurement setup followed the ISO standard 7626-2
[18]. The electrodynamic exciter: Vibration Exciter Type 4809 from B&K, was used to
excite the floor. An accelerometer of type 4367 and a force transducer of type 8200 both
from B&K were used to measure the input to the test element and the corresponding
output, respectively. A B&K Nexus type 2692 charge amplifier was used to enhance the
signal and sensitivity of both force transducer and accelerometer, respectively, and an
exciter amplifier was used to gain the shaker. The test setup can be seen in Figure 3.1.
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements 15
==PAGE:23==
Figure 3.1: Block diagram of the test setup for the structural reverberation time measurements. The figure
takes great inspiration in Figure 1 in ISO standard 7626-2 [18].
Initially, the setup was designed for 30 accelerometer positions and two shaker positions.
However, the number of shaker and accelerometer positions as well as the excitation
signal were adjusted after preliminary analysis of the results from mounting M0 and M1.0
(see mounting descriptions in Section 3.5). This preliminary analysis and the several
adjustments will be described later in this section.
Two different shaker mountings were used in the test setups: one in the workshop hall
and another in the laboratory. For mounting M0, installed in the workshop hall, the shaker
was suspended from the workshop hall crane with no support from the test element. For
the remaining mountings (see Section 3.5), the shaker was suspended with orange straps
from an iron rod clamped to two work stands, standing on the surrounding structure. In
both cases, the shaker was in contact with the test element only at the excitation point.
The two shaker mountings are shown in Figure 3.2.
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.2: Picture of the two shaker mountings. (a), shaker suspended from crane, (b), shaker suspended
from iron rod on working stands.
The connection between the shaker and the test element was the same for both shaker
mountings. The shaker was connected to the force transducer with a thin iron drive-rod,
and the force transducer was screwed to a washer, that was screwed to the floor with
16 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:24==
three screws (see Figure 3.3). The washer was unscrewed and then screwed back in for
every shift in shaker position.
Figure 3.3: Picture of the shaker and the force transducer connected by a thin drive-rod, secured to the floor
with a screwed-in washer.
As for mounting M9 and M10 (see mounting descriptions in Section 3.5), four washers
were attached to the concrete floor with adhesive instead of screws.
For the shaker mounting showed in Figure 3.2b a spirit level was used to horizontally
level the shaker. This was done to ensure that the force induced by the shaker on the
floor was as vertically aligned as possible. However, the floor was not perfectly horizon-
tal, in fact there was a noticeable deflection from the edges of the floor toward the center
(see Appendix 6), which introduced some inaccuracies in the measurements. To improve
accuracy and increase the coherence it was found, during the laboratory measurement
period, that the shorter the distance from shaker to force transducer, the better the accu-
racy.
Every accelerometer position were marked with small washers glued to the floor, and the
accelerometer was attached to the washers with a thin layer of bee wax. For mounting
M9 the accelerometer was attached directly to the floor with bee wax, because the small
washers hadn’t been glued to the floor yet. The accelerometer could still tightly attach
with beewax when placed directly on the floor.
This setup, with the shaker, drive rod, force transducer and accelerometer, corresponds
to configuration (b) in the ISO standard 7626-2, Figure 4 [18].
3.1.2
Software selection
Initially, it was considered whether PULSE 3560 or the room acoustic software Dirac 7.0,
both by B&K, would be best suitable for the structural reverberation time measurements.
PULSE features a two-channel measurement setup, suitable for cases where the source
impedance is comparable to that of the system under test. In such scenarios, the ex-
citation signal, s(t), differs from the input signal to the system, x(t) (see Figure 2.3 in
Section 2). It was assumed that this would be the case in the measurement setup for
this study, necessitating a two-channel measurement setup. Since PULSE is build with a
two-channel measurement setup, the software can provide real-time visualization of co-
herence graphs, auto-spectra for both force and acceleration, frequency response, and
impulse response during measurements. Thus, identification of errors in the measure-
ment setup such as misalignment of the shaker, too much background noise or improperly
attached accelerometers, were made possible. The large disadvantage with PULSE was
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements 17
==PAGE:25==
that only one accelerometer input was possible, hence making this measurement process
very time consuming.
Conversely, Dirac is primarily designed for room acoustic measurements, where the source
impedance is negligible compared to the system under test, necessitating only a one-
channel setup. The Dirac hardware had 16 available input channels, allowing simulta-
neous measurements with 16 accelerometers. However, due to the two-channel mea-
surement setup requirement, one input channel would have to be allocated to the force
transducer, one to the source input, and then five to the five available accelerometers.
This test setup configuration would significantly accelerate the measurement process, as
five positions could be measured simultaneously. However, it required additional post-
processing to obtain the coherence function and impulse response. The most significant
drawback from using Dirac was, that these plots could not be visualized during measure-
ments, thus, errors listed in the above paragraph, probably wouldn’t be identified, and
would possibly have resulted in many flawed measurements.
While Dirac could have expedited the measurement process, the ability to identify errors
during measurements was deemed more critical. Thus, PULSE was selected for struc-
tural impulse response measurements.
3.1.3
Calibration
The accelerometer were calibrated with an electrodynamic vibration calibrator 4294 from
B&K. The accelerometer were attached to the calibrator with beewax. The calibrator
produced a constant harmonic motion at 159.2 Hz with an acceleration of 10 m/s2. A
flat-top window was used, with 0% overlap and with a frequency resolution of df = 0.125.
The sensitivity was set to match the Nexus amplifier, and the gain was adjusted, so that
the autospectrum of the accelerometer at the peak was equal to 10 m/s2. The force
transducer was calibrated with the setup shown in Figure 3.4.
Figure 3.4: Picture of the calibration setup for the force transducer. From top to bottom: accelerometer,
weight, force transducer, shaker.
The calibrated accelerometer was used and a Mini Shaker Type 4810 was used for ex-
citation. With a weight of 102 grams and the accelerometer of 13 grams, the frequency
response should be:
18 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:26==
F = m · a ⇒a
F = 1
m = 10ms−2
F
=
1

(3.1)
using Newtons second law, where F is the force, a is the acceleration and m is the mass.
The sensitivity of the force transducer was set to match the Nexus amplifier, and the
gain was adjusted to reach approximately this value across the widest possible frequency
range in the frequency response.
Lastly, the measurement setup in Pulse was switched back to a hanning window with
maximum overlap, and the output from the accelerometer was integrated to get the ve-
locity, necessary for determining the impulse response. The sensitivity was increased for
mounting M9 and M10 after calibration on both the charge amplifier and in Pulse. This
was done due of smaller amplitude vibrations in the concrete floor.
3.1.4
Accelerometer- and shaker positions
The accelerometer and shaker positions were determined in collaboration with Melanie
Førsterling Gotfredsen, who needed to conduct the same measurements for her thesis:
”Vibro-acoustic prediction model for impact sound insulation of cross laminated timber
floors” [19]. The ISO standard 10848-1 Section 7.3 [20] demands at least three excitation
positions and at least three measurement positions for each excitation position. Further
requirements to the positioning of these excitation and measurement positions are also
stated here. However, the positioning grid was determined based on both the require-
ments from Gotfredsen’s study and to obtain additional data points for the investigations
in this study. The accelerometer positions were arranged in a 6x5 measurement grid and
marked with small washers glued to the floor. These accelerometer positions were kept
the same throughout this study. The accelerometer grid started 100 mm from each side of
a corner, and the positions were equidistantly spaced across the entire floor (see Figure
3.5).
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements 19
==PAGE:27==

are given in mm and the bold numbers next to each shaker position, are the shaker positions’ labelling number.
The black dotted line is the partition line of the two CLT panels (see Section 3.4.2. lw and ll are the equidistant
spacing in the accelerometer position grid in the length and in the width direction, respectively.
Each accelerometer position was labelled with a letter (A–F) and a number (1–5), as
shown in the figure above. Four shaker positions, labelled 1–4, were used in the project
and are also depicted in Figure 3.5.
Initially, the structural reverberation time was measured using shaker positions 1 and 2,
with all 30 accelerometer positions for each shaker position. It was clear from the results,
that the structural reverberation time varied a lot in especially the highest third-octave
bands (see Figure 3.6).

Besides coherence analysis, it was also of interest to investigate whether reverberation
time results differed significantly between accelerometer positions on the same and op-
posite floor panel, relative to the excitation point.
Figure 3.9: Boxplot of the estimated reverberation times in mounting M0 given in third-octave bands. The
blue boxes are shaker pos. 1 with acc. pos. on row A-C and shaker pos. 2 with acc. pos. on row D-F. The
red boxes are shaker pos. 1 with acc. pos. on row D-F and shaker pos. 2 with acc. pos. on row A-C.
The boxplots in Figure 3.9 show the difference in the results from the accelerometer po-
sitions on the same floor panel as the excitation point (blue boxes), and the ones on the
opposite floor panel to that of the excitation point (red boxes). From these plots it can be
22 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:30==
seen that the boxes, marking the 25th and 75th percentiles, in the third-octave bands from
2000 Hz and up, are not overlapping. This would indicate that the reverberation time in
these third-octave bands are significantly different from one another. It led to further anal-
ysis using a two-sample t-test with a significance level of 5%, to test the null hypothesis:
that the difference between reverberation times obtained from the accelerometer posi-
tions on the same floor panel as the excitation point and reverberation times obtained
from the accelerometer positions on the opposite floor panel as the excitation point has
a mean equal to zero. The null hypothesis was rejected in the third-octave bands: 50
Hz, 315 Hz, 630 Hz, 800 Hz, 1000 Hz, 1250 Hz, 1600 Hz, 2000 Hz, 2500 Hz, 3150 Hz,
4000 Hz, 5000 Hz, meaning that the reverberation time results from the the two different
accelerometer and shaker configurations would lead to significantly different results of the
structural reverberation time, with a significance level of 5%. The t-test was done with the
build-in function ttest2 in MATLAB.
Based on these preliminary results, it was decided to measure only at the accelerometer
positions on the same floor panel as the excitation point. Therefore, it was also deter-
mined to add two extra excitation positions, shaker positions 3 and 4, in order to have two
excitation positions for each floor partition, each with a set of 15 accelerometer positions.
Figure 3.10: Figure of the final shaker- and accelerometer position configuration used for the measurements
of the structural reverberation time. Only one shaker position was measured at a time. The dotted line is the
floor panel partition line and the bold numbers label the different shaker positions.
For the measurements conducted on the concrete floor in mounting M9 and M10, all 30
accelerometer positions were used with shaker positions 1 and 2 only.
3.1.5
Low frequency vs. Broadband excitation measurements
Initially, both broadband- and narrowband low frequency noise were used to excite the
floor mounting under test. The broadband noise spanned from 0-6400 Hz and the nar-
rowband low frequency from 0-400 Hz. The aim with the broadband measurements were
to get the frequency response over a wide frequency range. The low frequency measure-
ments were to get a good frequency resolution in the low frequency range aiming for clear
resonance peaks, whereby it would be possible to estimate the loss factor from the 3 dB
bandwidth method.
From the early results of the narrowband excitation measurements of mounting M0 and
M1.0 it could be seen that the measurements were too noisy to have a large enough
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements 23
==PAGE:31==
dynamic range to estimate the T20 reverberation times for each measurement position. A
dynamic range of at least 30 dB was necessary to estimate T20. From coherence plots,
a very low coherence was observed in the low frequency range, and therefore it was
suggested that sorting out the very low frequencies would help to increase the dynamic
range. The coherence graphs for shaker positions 1 and 2 and the corresponding 15
accelerometer positions can be seen for mounting M0 and M1.0 in Figure 3.11.
Figure 3.11: Early results of the coherence for mounting M0 and M1.0 for low frequency excitation measure-
ments. The x-axis spans from 0-100 Hz, showing the very low coherence in this region. The red crosses
mark the excitation position that was used for corresponding 15 accelerometer positions.
From the coherence plots it can be seen that the coherence is very low in the frequency
span of 0-100 Hz - especially for mounting M1.0. Therefore, filtering out the very low
frequencies was suggested to improve the measurement results.
The normalized impulse response and the energy-time-curves, ETCs, revealed that filter-
ing the measurement results with a MATLAB highpass filter with cut-off frequency at 35
Hz increased the dynamic range significantly.

Even though the dynamic range was increased with the highpass filter, the energy decay

The T20 estimates are obtained as described in Section 2.3.3. In the plots in Figure 3.14,
the EDCs are shown for all accelerometer positions with a large enough dynamic range.
Along with the EDCs, the plots show the marking points, -5 dB and -25 dB, from which the
linear inclined dotted line, is fitted to. These lines are extrapolated down to -60 dB, where
the intersection and estimated RT60 is marked with the vertical dotted lines. From Figure
3.14 it can be seen that there are only six curves with a large enough dynamic range to
estimate T20 in the 50 Hz third-octave band, and that the linear fit matches poorly with
the decay curves in the third-octave bands; 50 Hz, 63 Hz, 80 Hz, 160 Hz, 200 Hz, 250
Hz and 315 Hz - the decay curves should also appear linear. The same type of plot was
made for the broadband excitation measurements and can be seen in Figure 3.15.
26 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:34==
Figure 3.15: EDCs with RT60 estimates using T20, of broadband excitation measurements on mounting M0 for
the third-octave bands from 50 Hz to 315 Hz. The vertical dotted lines mark the reverberation time obtained
from the extrapolation of the linear fit between point -5 dB and -25 dB on the EDC down to -60 dB.
In this figure the linear fits matches the EDCs better in the third-octave bands; 63 Hz,
80 Hz, 200 Hz, 250 Hz, and 315 Hz, than they did for the low frequency excitation mea-
surements, showed in Figure 3.14. Thus, in this perspective the low frequency excitation
measurements wouldn’t provide any new or contributive information regarding the rever-
beration time, that couldn’t be obtained by the broadband excitation measurements.
In addition, one measurement average for the low frequency excitation measurements
took eight times as long as, one measurement average for the broadband excitation mea-
surements. This was due to the difference in frequency resolution. The low frequency
measurements took 8 s for each average, whereas the broadband frequency measure-
ments took 1 s. Approximately 50 averages were made for the low frequency excitation
measurements, in order to reach a steady coherence graph. This would add up to approxi-
mately 6 minutes and 30 seconds per accelerometer position to conduct the low frequency
excitation measurements, not to forget the extra time it took to change the measurement
template in Pulse. It was never considered to discard the broadband excitation measure-
ments, because the loss factor was wanted in the whole frequency range of interest in
building acoustic evaluations.
Based on the preliminary reverberation time analysis and the time considerations, it was
prioritized not to perform low frequency excitation measurements for the subsequent mea-
surements.
3.2
Sound insulation measurements
Impact and airborne sound insulation measurements were conducted on the various mount-
ings and mounting variations of the CLT floor (outlined in Section 3.5) following the ISO
standard series 10140-1:5 [3, 4, 5, 6, 7]. The measurement equipment were borrowed
from DTU and from FORCE Technology, and the followed measurement procedure was
instructed by Rasmus Stahlfest Holck Skov from FORCE Technology. Detailed descrip-
tions of each measurement- procedure and setup are provided in the following sections.
All measurements were conducted in the acoustic laboratories at DTU in Room 004 on
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements 27
==PAGE:35==
ground level and Room 904 in the basement. During some airborne and impact sound in-
sulation measurements, equipment from the structural reverberation time measurements
were present in Room 004. This was decided in order to spare time when switching be-
tween the two types of measurements. However, the extra equipment were reflected in
the measurements of sound insulation, i.e. in terms of the reverberation time, and no
equipment or material was added to nor removed from the rooms during a sound insula-
tion measurement series.
3.2.1
Airborne sound measurements
Airborne sound insulation was measured specifically in accordance with the ISO standard
10140-2. A Brüel & Kjær Type 2270 two-channel sound level meter was used with an air-
borne sound measurement template. Rotating booms were used both in the receiver- and
the source room, a GRAS 1” microphone was used in Room 004 and a B&K 1” microphone
as used in Room 904. Each microphone was connected to a pre-amplifier mounted on the
respective rotating booms, which were then connected to the junction boxes in Rooms 904
and 004, respectively, and connected to the B&K SLM in the nearby control room within
the building. No persons were present in the rooms during measurements. A sketch of
the setup is seen in Figure 3.16.
Figure 3.16: Figure sketch of the measurement setup in the laboratory Rooms 904 and 004, for airborne
sound insulation measurements. The corner loudspeaker in Room 004 was used for RT measurements, and
the omnidirectional loudspeaker in Room 904, was used for airborne sound insulation measurements.
The airborne sound insulation was measured from down to up, having the source room in
904 with a volume of approximately 243 m3, and the receiving room in 004 with a volume
of approximately 230 m3. Two fixed positions for the omnidirectional loudspeaker were
used, and pink noise was used, in order to have equal power in all third-octave bands.
The traverse period of the rotating boom was 64 s for reverberation time measurements
and 32 s for background- and airborne sound measurements. The microphones were cal-
ibrated prior to and after measurements, and the relative humidity, the room temperature
and the pressure was noted down approximately midway through measurements. Some
28 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:36==
background noise and reverberation time measurements were reused in measurements
conducted in continuation of one another within the same day. Measurement logs can be
seen in Appendix 6.
3.2.2
Impact sound measurements
Impact sound insulation measurements were conducted specifically in accordance with
the ISO standard 10140-3. The measurements were made in series with the airborne
sound measurements, and therefore most of the equipment and the test setup were the
same. The airborne sound measurement template was also used for the impact sound
measurements, enabling corrections for airborne sound in the post processing of the re-
sults. The test setup for impact sound measurements can be seen in Figure 3.17.
Figure 3.17: Figure sketch of the measurement setup in the laboratory Rooms 904 and 004, for impact sound
insulation measurements. The corner loudspeaker in Room 904 was used for RT measurements, and the
tapping machine in 004 for impact sound pressure level measurements.
The receiving room and the source room was opposite to the configuration for airborne
sound measurements, in order to have the tapping machine tapping on the floor under
test. A total of six tapping machine positions were used and placed at a 45 degree angle
to the direction of top layer of wooden planks of the CLT floor (see Appendix 6). The same
positions were used for the concrete floor in mounting M9 and M10.
Corrections for airborne sound transmission in the impact sound insulation measurement
results were conducted in accordance with ISO standard 10140-3 and 10140-4 and back-
ground noise corrections were made in accordance with ISO standard 10140-4. In Ap-
pendix 6, a table with the third-octave bands and the specific mounting to which correc-
tions have been applied can be seen. Furthermore, tables with the sound reduction index,
R, the normalized impact sound pressure level, Ln and the corresponding single-number
values, Rw and Ln,w, are presented for each mounting in all third-octave bands and can
be seen in Appendix 6.
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements 29
==PAGE:37==
3.3
Repeatability investigations of sound pressure level
measurements
The additional field of interest in this thesis study was regarding the uncertainty related to
measurements in the lower third-octave bands; 50 Hz, 63 Hz and 80 Hz. The investigation
assumed that increasing microphone positions would compensate for the spatial depen-
dency caused by the non-diffuseness of the sound field, enhancing result reproducibility
and reliability.
In order to investigate this further, it was decided to measure sound pressure levels in
Room 904 using six fixed microphones and two rotating booms randomly placed within
the distance requirements for measurement positions in the ISO standard series 10140.
The booms had a traverse period of 32 s. The source was an omnidirectional loudspeaker,
playing pink noise and the measurement period was 1 minute and 4 seconds with a build-
up time of 10 seconds. To enable simultaneous measurements across all eight channels,
the DEWEsoft software and hardware were used together with a tailored measurement
setup, which was provided by FORCE Technology. The DEWEsoft hardware was con-
nected via a cable to a computer located just outside room 904, resulting in the doors
being nearly fully closed.
In order to study the repeatability of sound pressure level measurements in the third-
octave bands 50 Hz, 63 Hz and 80 Hz, the sample size was carefully considered. A rule
of thumb in statistics is that a minimum of 30 samples are required for making statistical
sound conclusions [21]. Therefore, the following four different test setups were made:
1. 30 sound pressure level measurements in Room 904. Microphones positions were
not changed between measurement. Source position was in Room 904.
2. 30 sound pressure level measurements in Room 904. Microphones positions were
changed between each measurement. Source position was in Room 904.
3. 30 sound pressure level measurements in Room 904. Microphones positions were
not changed between measurement. Source position was in Room 004.
4. 30 sound pressure level measurements in Room 904. Microphones positions were
changed between each measurement. Source position was in Room 004.
The test setups with different source location can be seen in Figure 3.18.
30 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:38==
Figure 3.18: Figure sketch of the repeatability measurement test setups with loudspeaker position in Room
904 and 004, respectively.
The four different test setups allowed for comparison between measurement result ob-
tained with the source in Room 904 and 004, respectively. Additionally, the test setups
with anew microphone positions between measurements allowed for investigations on
whether or not this would lead to a higher spread in the results than if the microphones
remained in the exact same positions.
The measurements were conducted the 28th and 29th of November 2024 over a few
hours. A few background noise measurements were made in the beginning of each mea-
suring day in order to be able to correct for background noise in the post processing of
the measurement results. Pictures of the test setup can be seen in Figure 3.19.
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.19: Pictures from the the measurement setup in room 904 for repeatability tests of sound pressure
level measurements.

3.4
Preparation for laboratory measurements
Prior to conducting sound insulation measurements on the CLT floor, some preparations
of both the Room 904 and preparations to the floor itself needed to be made. These
preparations are described in the following sections.
3.4.1
Adjusting the reverberation time in room 904
Initially, the reverberation time in Room 904 was not correctly regulated in the lower third-
octave bands. According to the ISO standard 10140-5 [7], the reverberation time should
be
1 ≤T ≤2
( V
50
)2/3
,
(3.2)
for frequencies at and above 100 Hz. In the case of Room 904, the reverberation time
exceeded 2 seconds in most third-octave bands and especially the lower third-octave
bands, (50 Hz and 63 Hz), were extremely high. Therefore, several attempts at lowering
the reverberation time were made in terms of self-made absorbers of various types. The
requirement for the reverberation time in Room 904, with a volume of approximately 243
m3, would be:
1s ≤T ≤5.738s.
(3.3)
Even though not required by the ISO standard, the third-octave bands below 100 Hz were
also considered when adjusting the reverberation time. This was ensured because of the
repeatability investigations of the lowest third-octave frequency bands, (50 Hz, 63 Hz
and 80 Hz), that were scheduled later in the project plan. Therefore, it was considered
important that these lower third-octave bands as well met the requirements.
It took some trials to come up with an absorber design that would lower the reverberation
time enough to reach the requirements in the standard. A total of 12 absorber designs
and combinations were investigated, but the 50 Hz third-octave band proved particularly
challenging to reach. This was probably due to the lack of prior analysis, relying solely
on acoustic intuition and common sense for the designs. The final absorber setup, along
with the reverberation time results from Room 904 both before and after the final absorber
setup, can be seen in Figure 3.20a and 3.20b.
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.20: (a) Results of the reverberation time in Room 904, with the final absorber setup and as it was
originally, along with the ISO standard requirements of minimum and maximum reverberation time. (b) Picture
of the final absorber setup in Room 904.
32 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:40==
The added absorbers were the brown wooden box membrane absorber seen in the left
corner in Figure 3.20b and the black and grey porous absorber in the right corner of
the same figure. The wooden box were taken from the DTU laboratory deposit. The
solid wood sides were approximately 1.2 cm thick and the membrane in front was an
approximately 4 mm thin particle board plate. It was unknown whether any absorbent
material, apart from air, was put inside the box. The dimensions of the box were 590 x
1025 x 320 mm. The other absorber was a home-made porous absorber crafted on the
spot. It consisted of six 600 x 1200 x 150 mm ISOVER mineral wool blocks, placed side by
side, resulting in the total dimensions of 600 x 1200 x 900 mm. The sides were wrapped
in paperboard, the front was covered with thin black plastic, and the back was left open.
The absorber was assembled using duct tape. The two absorbers were positioned in
corners in order to absorb as much sound energy as possible. See Appendix 6 for a full
description of the tested absorber designs and combinations, along with pictures from the
lab and the results of the RT.
The final absorber setup for Room 904 was chosen, despite the results not being optimal.
Ideally, the reverberation time of the room should have been flat across the entire fre-
quency spectrum of interest, apart from an expected small gradual decrease towards the
higher frequencies. This scenario is ideal, because the aim of the room is to approximate
a perfectly diffuse sound field.
In Figure 3.20a, it can be seen that the reverberation time of the final design varies across
the measured frequency range, with a peak at 50 Hz, that is just below the ISO standard
requirement for maximum reverberation time. These results are not ideal, as some fre-
quencies will persist in the reverberation chamber for a longer time than others. However,
this absorber combination design was chosen because prioritizing compliance with ISO
standard requirements for reverberation time across all relevant third-octave bands was
deemed more important than achieving a flat RT curve.
In retrospective, time could have been saved by making a slit-absorber with calculated
dimensions, that would provide the slit-absorber to absorb at and around 50 Hz.
No changes were made to Room 004 as the reverberation time were above 1 s in all third-
octave bands both with and without present measurement equipment from the structural
reverberation time measurements.
3.4.2
Preparation of the floor
The CLT floor used for measurements in this study was in two panels. It was a seven-
layer floor assembly with layer thicknesses and total thickness as can be seen in Figure
3.21.
Figure 3.21: Detailed figure of the CLT floor used in this project. Measures are in mm. The width and the
number of layers in the width direction is not the true to the actual floor. The vertical dashed black line in the
midway through the figure marks the floor panel separation line.
The CLT floor was repurposed from a prior project by Artelia A/S: ”Fremtidens Biobaserede
Etagebyggeri” [22], for which it was originally produced.
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements 33
==PAGE:41==
Initially, the floor was found with a thin layer of some gray elastic material glued to most
of its top surface. This layer had to be removed prior to measurements to prevent mea-
surement errors caused by the softer surface. However, removing it was a slow and time
consuming process, hence only the accelerometer-, the shaker-, and the tapping machine
position areas were cleared. In Figure 3.22a and 3.22b the floor can be seen before and
after the elastic material was removed.
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.22: Picture of the CLT floor (a) prior to and (b) after parts of the thin gray elastic material glued to
its top surface was removed.
The floor was in two panels, a common configuration for CLT floors used in laboratory
measurements, with a vertically straight partition line as seen in Figure 3.21. The two
floor panels were joined using 29 screws equidistant along the partition line (see Figure
3.24a). This joint is amongst some commonly used joints in lab and in-situ [2, Figure 6.d].
The two floor panels were screwed together in the workshop hall prior to the first mounting
setup. As the screws were fastened the two panels aligned. A crane system, installed
in the workshop hall and laboratories, was used to lift the floor. The floor had 16 pass-
through lifting holes, that were located in pairs and aligned in two parallel lines across the
floor. Lifting straps were passed through the eight pairs of lifting holes and long iron pipes
were threaded through their loops, and secured to the yellow lifting beams of the crane
system with iron chains. The direction of the iron pipes were perpendicular to that of the
floor panel partition line, in order to be able to lift the floor in one piece (see Figure 3.23a
and 3.23b).
34 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:42==
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.23: Picture of the CLT floor lifted by the crane (a) and placed in the test opening (b).
After lifting the floor into the test opening, the iron pipes were removed and the floor panel
partition was thoroughly inspected. It was found that one of the floor panels was slightly
higher up than the other, so screws were unscrewed to realign the two floor panels. In
this process it was also found that two screws had broken during lifting.
After aligning the floor panels, the partition line was initially sealed with duct tape and
inspected for air leakage. Pink noise was set to play in the reverberation room below
(Room 904), and by listening to the floor for high frequency noise, it was found that the
duct tape seal was insufficient. As a result, the line was sealed with silicone sealant (see
Figure 3.24b).
(a)
(b)
(c)
(d)
Figure 3.24: Pictures (a) and (b) are of the screws joining the two CLT floor panels (a), and silicone sealant
in the floor panel partition line and lead rubber taped on top of the lifting holes (b). Pictures (c) and (d) are of
the lead rubber material used for air sealing (c), and the lifting holes stuffed with mineral wool (d).
The lifting holes were also sealed for airtightness. To ensure a proper seal, mineral wool
was stuffed into the holes and initially sealed with duct tape. However, in the same way as
the sealing process for the partition line, it was found that this proved insufficient. Instead,
a layer of lead rubber was placed on top of the holes and taped to the floor with duct tape
(see Figures 3.24b, 3.24c, and 3.24d). In a final check for air leakage it was found that
high-frequency noise was still audible through the floor, coming from several medium to
larger floor cracks. One crack was covered with duct tape, while the others were left
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements 35
==PAGE:43==
open. Early results from the airborne sound insulation measurements showed no visible
air leakage, and therefore, no further actions were taken to air-tighten the floor.
3.5
Mountings
As CLT floor mountings are currently done differently worldwide across the acoustic labo-
ratories, several different mounting conditions have been outlined in this study and inves-
tigated in terms of sound insulation and loss factor. These mounting conditions reflect the
known mounting deviations between acoustic laboratories, as well as additional plausible
floor mountings and concrete floor reference measurements.
The mountings have been divided into main mountings and mounting variations. Main
mountings includes mountings where larger changes to the coupling between the test el-
ement and the surrounding structure has been made. The change in test element, i.e.
CLT floor and concrete floor, is also considered as separate main mountings. The mount-
ing variations are smaller variations made to the main mounting. A mounting variation,
for example, refers to whether the floor is unloaded or loaded with 25 kg/m2. To provide
the reader with an overview of the main mountings Table 3.1 has been made.

Rw & Ln represents impact and airborne sound insulation measurements, and Ts is measurements of the
