The labelling system of the different mountings is as follows: M(for mounting).Mounting
Number.Mounting Variation. Thus, M1.3 refers to mounting one, variation three. Detailed
descriptions of the mountings are provided later in this section.
Mounting nine and ten are measurements conducted on the 140 mm concrete ISO stan-
dard reference floor [7], which is rather a floor change than a different mounting. However,
they follow the same labelling structure as for the different mountings of the CLT floor to
maintain simplicity and consistency in the table.
The following sections provide a detailed description of each tested mounting condition.
Mounting M0
Initially, a setup that approximated simple support conditions was designed, with the CLT
floor resting on eight cone-shaped brass elements in the workshop hall. This setup was
included in order to be able to separate the internal losses, happening in the floor itself,
from the coupling losses introduced by the specific mounting of the floor in the laboratory
test-opening. The brass cones had a top diameter of 1 cm and was placed on a concrete
floor in a rectangular formation, supporting both CLT floor panels close by their respective
corners.
Figure 3.25: Picture of the simple support mounting setup, of the CLT floor. Eight cone-shaped brass ele-
ments, of which two is visible in the image, are supporting the corners of the both floor panels.
38 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:46==
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.26: (a) Figure sketch of the simple support mounting setup of the CLT floor. Eight cone-shaped
brass elements supports each floor panel corner. (b) Picture of the cone-shaped brass element.
This setup was not an ideal simple support setup, since the floor was not fixed to the
cones and the cones were not pointed. The latter was due to practicalities as it would
damage the floor to have it resting on points rather than on small surface areas. How-
ever, these deviations from the ideal simple support case, was considered negligible in
the measurement results and in the further analysis of estimating the floor’s internal loss
factor.
Mounting M1
In the mounting setup for mounting M1, the CLT floor was placed directly within the metal
frame, which enclosed the floor test opening in the acoustic laboratories at DTU (see
Figure 3.27a and 3.27b).
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.27: Mounting M1. (a) Picture of the floor opening and the metal frame in which the CLT floor was
placed directly. (b) Figure sketch of the structural coupling between floor and frame.
The floor was loosely placed within the frame, connected to the surrounding structure via
the seating surface of the metal frame, the mineral wool in the vertical gap between floor
and frame, and the duct tape used for air sealing.
Mounting M2
Mounting M2 was very similar to M1 except that for this setup the floor was placed on
one layer of mineral wool strips loosely placed on the entire seating surface of the metal
frame.
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements 39
==PAGE:47==
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.28: Mounting M2. (a) Picture of the floor opening with one layer of mineral wool strips loosely placed
on the seating surface of the metal frame and on which the CLT floor was placed. (b) Figure sketch of the
structural coupling between floor and frame.
This mounting was tested in order to investigate the effect of smoothening the roughness
between the metal frame and the floor, ensuring contact between the floor and the frame
around the entire floor perimeter. Similar to mounting M1, the floor was loosely placed in
the test opening.
Mounting M3
Mounting M3 deviated from the first two described mountings, since it was fastened in the
test opening with long wooden wedges around the entire perimeter. However, similar to
mounting M2, the floor was placed on one layer of mineral wool strips.
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.29: Mounting M3. (a) Picture of the CLT floor in the test opening fastened with long wooden wedges
around the entire floor perimeter. (b) Figure sketch of the structural coupling between floor and frame.
The wedges wasn’t in one piece along an entire floor side, but were cut into smaller parts,
for practical reasons (see pictures of the long wooden wedges in Appendix 6. The wedges
were hammered in to fixate the floor. The angle of the wedge inclination was 8 degrees,
matching the angle inclination of the metal frame and thereby ensuring a tighter fit and a
better fastening of the floor in the metal frame. However, the fit was imperfect in some
areas for various reasons.
Firstly, the unevenness and roughness of the floor structure and the metal frame, com-
bined with a not perfectly positioned floor in the test opening, added up to the fact, that
some wooden wedges would tightly fit in one spot and be loose in another. No matter
how much the wedge was hammered down, it would still be able to tilt from left to right if
force was applied. Therefore, it proved better, in the sense of having a tight connection
around the entire perimeter, to have the wedges cut into smaller pieces instead of having
one long wedge for fully covering each side of the floor.
40 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:48==
Secondly, at some locations the gap between the floor and the metal frame was simply
too large. In these cases, small plastic wedges were used in combination with the wooden
wedge to fill out the gap and ensure a tight fit. Corners were filled with mineral wool and
so was the gap between the metal frame and the floor beneath the wooden wedges. The
plastic wedges were cut to align with the top surface of the wooden wedge in order to
make it easier to seal afterwards with duct tape.
Some of the above listed issues might be avoided in the future if the wooden wedges are
factory-made rather than handmade, as they were in this project.
Mounting M4, M5 and M6
Mounting M4, M5, and M6 were variations of mounting M3. For these mountings, shorter
wooden wedges replaced the longer ones constituting fixation points rather than fixation
lines. Additionally, using fewer short wedges would simplify the mounting process and
reduce material usage, which for practical reasons would be desirable in a future per-
spective. The short wooden wedges was approximately 5 cm wide, and they were cut
from the previously used long wooden wedges.
(a) Mounting M4.
(b) Mounting M5.
(c) Mounting M6.
Figure 3.30: Figures of mountings M4:6. The mountings vary in the amount of short wooden wedges used
to fixate the floor in the metal frame. The horizontal thin black line in the middle of each figure, shows the
partition line between the two floor panels.
The mountings M4:6 varied with increasing numbers of short wooden wedges in the gap
between the metal frame and the floor. Mounting M4 had fewest wedges to fixate the floor
in the test opening, with eight fixation points on each side of the floor corners. Mounting M5
had two extra fixation points in the joint between the two floor panels, aiming to eliminate
an additional degree of freedom from the floor when vibrating. The final variation, M6,
included eight additional fixation points; two evenly spaced on each side of the floor. The
main focus of these mountings was to examine how a gradual increase in fixation points
— from the minimum required to secure the floor to full lines along the entire perimeter
— would affect the loss factor. Similar to mounting M3, plastic wedges was added at
locations where the gap between the metal frame and the floor was too large, and mineral
wool was stuffed both beneath- and in-between the wedges.
The wedges were hammered down, fixating the floor in the frame tightly. However, this
introduced complications during the demounting process, as some wedges became stuck
and could not be removed without breaking. In Appendix 6 pictures of the plastic- and
wooden wedges, whole and broken, can be seen. Sound insulation measurements were
not conducted on M5 and M6, since having two to ten more fixation points was considered
to have a minimal effect.
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements 41
==PAGE:49==
Mounting M7
For mounting M7, the floor was casted-in in the metal frame. To cast in the floor a mixture
of Weber Tørbeton 0-8, gravel and water was used, which in the following will be referred
to as mortar. The gravel was added to the mixture, to ease the demounting process of
the floor. The gap between the metal frame and the floor was hardly stuffed with mineral
wool up to approximately 3 cm below the top surface of the floor. The mortar was then
added and aligned to the top surface of the floor, and left to dry for at least three days. To
ease the demounting process further, the metal frame was lubricated with sunflower oil
and a layer of plastic was put between the floor and the metal frame to prevent moisture
from the wet mortar from being absorbed into the wood during the drying period.
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.31: Mounting M7. (a) Picture of the CLT floor in the test opening, placed on one layer of mineral
wool strips and fixed with mortar. (b) Figure sketch of the structural coupling between floor and frame.
Compared to mounting M3, this mounting was also fixated along the perimeter, however,
in contrast to M3, where the long wooden wedges were loose at some locations allowing
single wedges to tilt back and forth, the casting-in of the floor in the frame ensured contact
along the entire perimeter. This mounting was, likewise the previous mountings M2:7,
placed on 1 layer of mineral wool strips. From mounting M2:7 the floor was not removed
from test opening.
Mounting M8
Mounting M8 was resting on one layer of VIKAFOAM. VIKAFOAM is a polyurethane ma-
terial that comes in many variants with varying hardnesses. The specific product used in
the study was called VIKAFOAM 26 and had a thickness of 12.5 mm. With a width of 5
cm, the foam did not cover the whole seating surface of the metal frame, but was placed
as close to the inner edge of the frame as possible, and cut to fit this perimeter. In this
way, any arbitrary folding of the material were avoided when the floor was hoisted down
on it. A picture of VIKAFOAM can be seen in Appendix 6.
42 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:50==
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.32: Mounting M8. (a) Picture of the CLT floor in the test opening, laying loose on one layer of
VIKAFOAM. (b) Figure sketch of the structural coupling between floor and frame.
For this mounting the floor was loosely placed in the test opening and the frame-floor gap
was filled with mineral wool. The point of interest for this mounting was to investigate the
effect of having the floor lying on an elastic layer - a mounting scenario that is used in-situ
[2].
The CLT floor had been lifted twice (from the test opening and back again into the test
opening) in order to place the VIKAFOAM in the metal frame for the M8 mounting. The
floor was not inspected for broken screws after mounting, but a fresh layer of silicone
sealant was applied over the cracked old sealant to prevent from air leakage.
Mounting M9 and M10
The last two mountings were, as mentioned earlier, made on the 140 mm concrete ISO
standard reference floor, with the purpose of having a reference value of the loss factor for
later analysis. Since plenty of sound insulation measurements on the 140 mm concrete
reference floor have been made and reported by lots of acousticians prior to this study,
these measurements were not conducted.
(a)
(b)
(c)

Figure 3.33: Mounting M.9 and M.10. Picture and figure sketches of the 140 mm concrete floor loosely
mounted (a and c) and rigidly mounting (b and d), and both placed on one layer of mineral wool strips in the
metal frame of the test opening.
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements 43
==PAGE:51==
Mounting M9 was, likewise mounting M2, placed loosely in the metal frame on one layer
of mineral wool strips. The gap between the metal frame and the floor was stuffed with
mineral wool and sealed with duct tape. Mounting M10 was placed on one layer of mineral
wool strips and cast in place with the same procedure as for mounting M7.
The four iron eye bolts attached to the floor in mounting M9 were removed in mounting
M10 due too other experiments made on the floor by other clients of the DTU acoustic
laboratories in between the mountings. The eye bolts were not reattached to the floor by
mistake, but were also considered not to be of major influence on the loss factor results.
3.5.1
Mounting variations
Four mounting variations were outlined and consistent across all main mountings. The
variations were combinations of two parameters; with and without loading of 25 kg/m2 on
the floor and with and without lead rubber as edge covering. Six out of ten main mountings
were measured with all four variations, whereas the remaining four main mountings were
measured with only a few of the variations.
The four mounting variations can be seen in Figure 3.34a to 3.34d.
(a) Mounting variation 0.
(b) Mounting variation 1.
(c) Mounting variation 2.
(d) Mounting variation 3.
Mounting M0 was made only with the simplest variation, variation 0, as the purpose of
this mounting was to measure the internal loss factor of the floor. Therefore, impacts from
external factors was minimized as greatly as possible.
Mounting M5 and M6 were measured in terms of structural reverberation time and solely
with floor variation 1. These mountings were measured to investigate how gradually in-
44 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:52==
creasing the number of fixation points, would influence the loss factor, and it was consid-
ered that the lead rubber variations would not have that big an impact on the loss factor.
Variation 1 was chosen to measure on, since early results from the previous mountings
showed, that this mounting variation might be of more relevance. These choices were
made due to time constraints and hard prioritization.
Loss factor measurements were made on the two reference mountings, M9 and M10, with
variation 0 and 1. Time constraints weren’t an issue at this time, and both variations were
of interest.

==PAGE:53==
4
Results and Analysis
The mountings with the mounting variations have been measured in terms of structural
reverberation time and airborne- and impact sound insulation and the results and analy-
sis follows in this section. Table 3.2 provides an overview of the measurements that has
been conducted on the various mounting- and mounting variations. Given the consider-
able amount of measurements that have been conducted throughout this project, many
results have been left out in this section in order to keep it short and concise. The results
excluded from this section, are included in the several appendices, and referred to where
appropriate.
4.1
Loss factors
The loss factor (in third-octave bands) was determined by measuring the structural re-
verberation time and subsequently calculating the loss factor using Formula 2.13. The
results from the mounting variations without weight loading can be seen in Figure 4.1.
The minimum requirement for the loss factor as prescribed in the ISO standard 10140-5
[7] have been included in the same figure. Lastly, the loss factor values for mounting M1.0
in the third-octave bands; 80 Hz, 100 Hz and 125 Hz has been estimated with the 3 dB
bandwidth method by Gotfredsen [19] and are included in the figure for comparison of the
two methods.
Figure 4.1: Loss factor in third-octave bands for the mounting and mounting variations without weight loading.
*) are values obtained from Gotfredsen [19]. ηmin_ISO is the minimum loss factor requirement from the ISO
standard 10140-5 [7].
From these results it can be seen that the loss factor values are very similar across the
several mountings. As expected, Figure 4.1 shows that the deviations between mounting
variations 0 and 2 (without and with edge covering) were small. This would be due to the
46 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:54==
minimal impact the lead rubber edge covering would have on the coupling between the
CLT floor and the surrounding structure. Mounting M8 deviates from the other mountings
in the 63 Hz third-octave band, where it has a large sharp peak.
Furthermore, the results show that mounting M3 and M7 has a higher loss factor than
the other mountings in the mid-frequency range. Mounting M3 shows a higher loss factor
than all mountings (except from M7) from 160 Hz to 800 Hz. Mounting M7 has the highest
loss factor from 80 Hz to 2000 Hz, except for the peak observed in mounting M8 in the
63 Hz third-octave band. This would be due to the fixation of the floor in the metal frame
with long wooden wedges for mounting M3 and with mortar for mounting M7. The wider
ranging higher loss factor for mounting M7 can be explained by a more uniform fixation
along the entire perimeter, enabled by casting the floor directly into the metal frame.
The loss factor values obtained with the 3 dB bandwidth method (from Gotfredsen) ap-
pears to be lower than the ones obtained with the structural reverberation time. However,
both results follow the same curve shape. The 3 dB bandwidth method is sensitive to
poor coherence and frequency resolution, which explains why only the loss factor results
for the three third-octave bands shown in Figure 4.1 were obtainable. Similarly it could
be the explanation to the deviations in the results. However, the exact cause of these
deviations are still uncertain.
In Figure 4.2 a similar plot to that in Figure 4.1 have been made. However, only mounting
variations 0 and 1 (without and with loading) have been plotted, in order to investigate the
difference between these two mounting variations. As observed in Figure 4.1, the lead
rubber had a minimal impact on the loss factor. Therefore, mounting variations 2 and 3
have been excluded from this figure to maintain clarity.
Figure 4.2: Loss factor in third-octave bands for the mounting and mounting variations with- (dotted) and
without (solid) loading. *) are values obtained from Gotfredsen [19]. ηmin_ISO is the minimum loss factor
requirement from the ISO standard 10140-5 [7].
From the figure it can be seen that by loading the floor the loss factor increases in the
third-octave bands from 80 Hz to 630 Hz. This can be explained by the friction occurring
between the weights and the floor as the floor vibrates, leading to dissipation of energy in
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements 47
==PAGE:55==
the form of heat. The same tendencies observed for mountings M3.0 and M7.0 in Figure
4.1 are also observable for mountings M3.1 and M7.1 in Figure 4.2. Similarly, the higher
loss factor is due to the fixation of the floors, and the fixation with mortar affects a broader
frequency range.
In addition the minimum requirement is used for comparison for the CLT floor even though
the requirement only applies to heavier structures (m > 150kg/m2). The surface of the
CLT floor is m = 110kg/m2. It is seen that all mountings, except for mounting M1.0,
meets the minimum loss factor requirement from the ISO standard 10140-5. Mounting
M1.0 fails to meet the third-octave band 315 Hz requirement, deviating by 0.001 from the
minimum threshold. However, one could argue that with this third decimal deviation the
requirements are still met for mounting M1.0.
Further investigations to the ISO standard minimum requirement has been made. As
described in the theory section with Equation 2.12, the minimum requirement is based on
an assumed internal loss factor of 0.01. Therefore, the internal loss factor of the CLT floor
was investigated with the mounting on simple supports, M0. Likewise mounting M1.0, the
loss factor was estimated both from the structural reverberation time and from the 3 dB
bandwidth method performed by Gotfredsen.
Figure 4.3: Internal loss factor estimations of the CLT floor from mounting M0. *) are values obtained from
Gotfredsen and the black dashed line is the estimated internal loss factor constant obtained from Gotfredsen
[19]. The mountings in gray tone are for comparison to total loss factor estimations.
The results reveal still large deviations in the low third-octave bands between the loss
factor values obtained by the two different methods. Considering the estimation method
using structural reverberation time, it is doubtful that the internal loss factor of the CLT floor
(from M0) would be higher than the total loss factors for the other mountings, which is seen
to be the case in the third-octave bands of 50 Hz and 63 Hz. Additionally, simulations from
Gotfredsen’s study showed that an internal loss factor constant of ηint = 0.02 was a good
estimate. Therefore, this internal loss factor constant has been used in this study for
further analysis.
If ηint = 0.02, specifically determined for the CLT floor, were to be used instead of the
48 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:56==
ISO standard value of ηint = 0.01 in the equation for the minimum requirement, not many
mountings would meet the requirement. Equation 2.12 used with ηint = 0.02 is referred
to as the adjusted ISO standard requirement hereinafter.
(a)
(b)
(c)
Figure 4.4: Figure tables of loss factor values for all CLT mountings and variations in every evaluated third-
octave band. The red coloured cells contain loss factor values that are less than the adjusted ISO standard
requirement using ηint = 0.02 instead of ηint = 0.01 in figure (a). Figure (b) and (c) are the adjusted
requirement -10% and +10%, respectively.
The exact numbers in these tables are not of specific relevance but the colored and uncol-
ored cells in the Figure 4.4a shows that M7.1 and M7.3 are the only mountings that meets
the adjusted requirements in all third-octave bands from 50 Hz to 4000 Hz. No mount-
ing meets the adjusted requirements in the 5000 Hz third-octave band. Figures 4.4b and
4.4c include a ±10% uncertainty margin for the adjusted loss factor requirements, as the
internal loss factor of the CLT floor is only an estimate, and thereby comes with uncer-
tainty. From Figure 4.4b it can be seen that even with a -10 % margin only mounting M7
succeeds in meeting the requirements and in Figure 4.4c it is seen that only M7.3 meets
the adjusted requirements.
It should be clear that the adjusted requirements are not ISO standard requirements,
nor is the CLT floor encompassed by the standing ISO standard requirements, as the
requirements only apply for heavier floors. This minimum requirement analysis serves
solely to provide perspective on the measured loss factor results.
Therefore, lastly it has been investigated how well the 140 mm concrete floor meets the
ISO standard requirements.
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements 49
==PAGE:57==
Figure 4.5: Loss factor in third-octave bands for the mounting M9 and M10 with mounting variations 0 and 1.
Few loss factor results from the CLT floor are given in gray tone and showed for comparison to the concrete
floor. ηminISO is the minimum requirement from the ISO standard 10140-5.
In Figure 4.5 it can be seen that the loss factor for the two concrete floor mountings are
below the ISO standard minimum requirements in numerous third-octave bands in the
mid-high frequency area. Furthermore, it can be observed that the added load doesn’t
have that big an impact on the total loss factor for both mountings. It was unexpected that
the standard 140 mm concrete floor did not satisfy the minimum requirement, and even
more so that casting it in (i.e. mounting M10) did not result in a loss factor exceeding the
minimum requirement.
4.2
Impact- and airborne sound insulation
Prior to looking at the airborne- and impact sound insulation results, the critical frequency
as well as the first couple of modes in the CLT floor has been calculated. Central val-
ues used in the calculations in this current section and in Section 4.2.3 can be seen in
Appendix 6.
4.2.1

The critical frequency of the CLT floor has been calculated using Equation 2.6.

From this it can be seen that the mode, f11, where m = n = 1, is larger than the critical
frequency (f11 > fc), which is typically seen for small thick plates [8]. This is consistent
with the CLT floor having a thickness of h = 240mm, which can be considered as relatively
thick for a structural building component. Furthermore, the thickness and the lightness of
the CLT floor, with msurface = 110.496kg/m2, both are indicators of a low critical frequency
of the floor.
Additionally, the first couple of modes in the thickness direction of the CLT floor was anal-
ysed, due to its relatively large thickness. It was suspected that these resonances might
fall within the measured frequency range, thereby affecting the airborne sound insulation
of the floor. The modes were calculated using Equation 2.8 and can be seen in Table
4.2. The wave speed in the thickness direction, used to calculate these modes, has been
obtained from the study; ”Elastic wave modes for the assessment of structural timber:
Ultrasonic echo for building elements and guided waves for pole and pile structures” from
2014 [24]. The wave speed used is cz = 1550m/s, determined from the mean value of the
measured wave speeds in the thickness direction in the referenced study (see Appendix
6 for the detailed calculation).
4.2.2
Sound insulation measurement results
Impact- and airborne sound insulation measurements were measured on the mountings
and mounting variations as showed in Table 3.2. To simplify the plots, only mounting
variations 0 and 1 are presented in this section, as the effect of adding lead rubber over
the edges was minimal due to the poor sound insulation of the CLT floor. Furthermore, the
curve pattern for mounting variations 0 and 1 (without edge covering) were similar to that
of variations 2 and 3 (with edge covering). As expected, loading the floor influenced the
sound insulation results, justifying the inclusion of both mounting variations in Figure 4.6.
All measurement results in the form of table values and graphs are provided in Appendix
6. The same appendix also includes markings of background noise and airborne sound
corrections.

In the Figures 4.6a and 4.6b many similarities can be seen between each individual mount-

y-axis on both plots are ranging 60 dB, leaving the plots with much white space. How-
ever, this is the ISO standard prescribed way of showing sound insulation measurement
results.
In Figure 4.6a there are three clear dips around the third-octave bands; 63-80 Hz, 160
Hz and at 2500-3150 Hz, indicating poor sound insulation at these frequencies. The dips
around the 63-80 Hz third-octave band would be a combination of the critical frequency
and the mode numbers f10 and f11 (see Table 4.1). Likewise, the dips around 160 Hz
would be due to the resonance at f02. The dip in sound reduction index around the third-
octave bands 2500 Hz and 3150 Hz is probably due to the first resonance in the thickness
direction, although the theoretically calculated resonance frequency is slightly higher (see
Table 4.2). However, it should be noted that the wave speed in the thickness direction
were an approximation.
The peaks in Figure 4.6b, indicating poor sound insulation, are a little less clear, however
still visible, in the same third-octave bands as in Figure 4.6a. Yet another two peaks are
visible at 250 Hz and 400 Hz respectively, which would also be due to resonances.
In both figures, mounting M8 deviates from the other mountings in the third-octave bands
from approximately 200 Hz and below. This might be caused by the interaction between
the CLT floor and the VIKAFOAM causing the system to resonate differently.
It was expected that the sound insulation of the floor would improve by loading the floor.
This effect can indeed be seen in the Figures 4.7a and 4.7b where the mounting M8 has

In Table 4.3 it can be seen that the mounting that generally performs best in both airborne

4.2.3
Theoretical airborne- and impact sound insulation of the floor.
In order to theoretically determine the sound reduction index of the CLT floor, the radiation
efficiency from forced and resonant vibrations were calculated with the equations from
Section 2.2.2 over the frequency span of 31.5 Hz to 5000 Hz. The radiation efficiencies
can be seen in Figure 4.8a and the theoretical total loss factor can be seen in Figure 4.8b.
(a)
(b)
Figure 4.8: Theoretical results for the CLT floor of (a) the resonant radiation efficiency and (b) the theoretical
total loss factor with X = 0.3.
It is seen from the Figure 4.8a that the resonant radiation efficiency aligns well with the
theory for thick small plates where f11 > fc (see Figure 2.1). The total loss factor was
calculated as outlined in Section 2.2.1 with X = 0.3, and the internal loss factor used in
the calculations were the estimated constant of ηint = 0.02 obtained from Gotfredsen’s
54 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:62==
study. It can be seen in Figure 4.8b, that the theoretical loss factor aligns well with the
measured loss factors for mountings M3.0 and M7.0 (floor rigidly fixated in metal frame)
and is a bit of an overestimate in the mid-frequency span of approximately 100 Hz to 1250
Hz for the remaining mountings.
As described in the theory section the theoretical R and Ln are derived from SEA and
some of the general fundamental assumptions proved not to hold for the CLT floor. This
regards assumptions about the modal density that should be high and smooth and the
diffuseness of the sound field. In Table 4.4 the statistically and manually counted modes
as well as the the modal overlap for the third-octave bands from 31.5 to 1000 can be seen.

From the table it can be seen that the modal overlap is much lower than 1, which reveals
that the modes that are present in their respective third-octave bands are dominating. In
SEA the modal overlap should be far above 1 in order to be able to consider the sound
field to be diffuse. Furthermore, it is seen that in the third-octave bands below 800 Hz,
the mode count is less or equal to 3. In SEA the number of modes in each third-octave
band should be above 1 and preferable above 3. Thereby, the modal density cannot be
considered high and smooth in these third-octave bands and consequently, the modal
density in these third-octave bands was determined from the manually counted modes
and used in the subsequent calculation of the theoretical R and Ln. Above 800 Hz the
statistical modal density for bending waves were used, and in the third-octave bands
above fs/2 = 2165Hz the statistical modal density for shear waves were used.
The forced and resonant transmission losses were obtained from the equations outlined
in Section 2.2.3, and can be seen in Figure 4.9.
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements 55
==PAGE:63==
Figure 4.9: Theoretical transmission loss, R, and normalized impact sound pressure level, Ln along with
measurement results from mounting M2.0 for comparison. Ln (stiff floor force) is calculated with Equation
2.22 and Ln is calculated with Equation 2.21.
The sharp peaks in R and sharp dips in Ln result from third-octave bands with no modes
present, as explained from the equations in the theory section (see Equations 2.19 and
2.21).
As it can be seen from Figure 4.9 the theoretical results doesn’t match perfectly with the
measured results. The peaks in the theoretical R are shifted upwards compared to RM2.0
and there is an extra peak in the theoretical R at 315 Hz. The resonance in the thickness
direction is not visible in the theoretical R, which is evident as they are not used in deriving
the SEA formulas. The theoretical R are similar to the RM2.0 in the third-octave bands from
400 Hz to 2000 Hz, where the curve shape follows that of the mass law. However, the
RM2.0 is about 7 dB lower in this frequency area, which would be due to the finiteness of
the structure. The mass law for normal incidence is derived from the consideration of an
infinite plate. Taking the finite size into account R is approximately 5 dB lower.
The theoretical Ln is much lower that of the measured Ln,M2.0. As for R and RM2.0, the
theoretical Ln is shifted upwards but has similar dips as Ln,M2.0 from around 315 Hz and
above. The Ln (stiff floor force), corresponding to the force on a heavy, stiff floor, is seen
to be slightly higher than the Ln calculated using Gotfredsen’s force. The curve shape of
the measured Ln,M2.0 from around 500 Hz and above is seen to match the curve shape of
the theoretical Ln (force from Gotfredsen).
Lastly it should be noted that the theoretical results generally predict better sound insula-
tion than the measured results.

Repeatability investigations of sound pressure level

Therefore, the analysis is somewhat deficient and rather superficial. However, all results

The analysis made in this study limits itself to the graphs seen in Figure 4.10. The four sub-

described in Section 3.3, which are reiterated here to reduce confusion.
1. 30 sound pressure level measurements in Room 904. Microphones positions were
not changed between measurement. Source position was in Room 904.
2. 30 sound pressure level measurements in Room 904. Microphones positions were

In each of the aforementioned figures, the results from the eight microphone channels

microphone positions, the plotted results are the energetic mean of the result from these
six microphones. Along with the results from each microphone category, the arithmetic

In all sub-figures a combined plot of the means and standard deviations from the three
microphone categories has been made for comparison.
Since the plots for measurements with anew microphone positions between measure-

the former is shown in large scale for space considerations, while the latter is presented
in a smaller scale.
Background noise corrections were made from the arithmetic mean of all background
noise measurements and no correction for background noise was required. It should be
noted that the loudspeaker output remained constant regardless of its placement either in
Room 904 or 004, consequently leading to lower sound pressure levels when the source
was positioned in Room 004.

becoming more and more ideal. From Figure 4.10a and 4.10b it can be seen that the

results obtained from the rotating in the third-octave bands of 50 Hz and 63 Hz. Addi-

figures the standard deviation interval for the standing microphones is approximately 5 dB
and 8 dB. These observations are very approximate and a proper statistical investigation
on difference significance is required in order to draw conclusions from this. The spread
in the results are very similar between all microphone categories in both Figures from
around 80 Hz to 2500 Hz.
In both Figures deviations in the measurement results between the standing microphones
and the rotating booms can be seen in the third-octave bands above 2500 Hz, where the
standing microphones appear to be measuring lower sound pressure levels than the ro-
tating booms. The reason for this is uncertain, but it might have to do with the directivity of
the microphones. All standing microphones were pointing upwards, whereas the direction
of the microphone on the rotating booms are uncertain.
Despite the reason for this deviation the results reveal a possibility to influence the re-
sults from a choice measurement equipment. The results suggests that airborne sound
insulation measurements conducted with a rotating boom in the receiving room and fixed
microphone positions in the sending room, or vice versa, would lead to a lower sound
reduction index and a higher sound reduction index in these third-octave bands, respec-
tively. Although, the single-number values wouldn’t be particularly affected by this devia-
tion as these values are determined from a reference curve ranging from 100 Hz to 3150
Hz.

4.4
Sources of Error
During and after laboratory measurements certain sources of error was detected. These
sources of error have been divided into two groups: one related to errors introduced by
applying filters, and the other concerning site events that contaminated the conducted
measurements.
4.4.1
Effect of accelerometer positioning on structural reverberation time
measurements
The 6×5 measurement position grid and the shaker positions used in the structural rever-
beration time measurements was outlined in Section 3.1.4. However, not all requirements
given in ISO 10848-1, Section 7.3.3 [20] for structural reverberation time measurements
were fulfilled. Specifically, the minimum distance requirements were violated. The re-
quirement states that for measurement positions there should be at least 0.5 m to the
floor edges. In this study the distance from the outer accelerometer positions to the floor
edge was approximately 10 cm. Additionally, the four accelerometer positions closest to
the excitation points were all less than 0.5 m away - also violating the ISO standard re-
quirements. Due to these violations of standardized measurement procedures, it was of
interest to investigate the effect of this on the measurement results.
For the purpose of evaluating the effect of violating the standardized measurement proce-
dure, colormaps was made for all structural reverberation time measurements. In Figure
4.12 the colormaps for mounting M1.1 are presented. These colormaps are presented
here solely to provide a visual representation, while the colormaps for the remaining
mountings can be found in Appendix 6.
60 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:68==
Figure 4.12: Colormap of all accelerometer positions and their respective modified Z-scores for the structural
reverberation time measurements of mounting M1.1. The colormaps have been made for the third-octave
bands with the same center frequency as the corresponding octave band. Shaker positions are marked with
their respective label number and the dashed lines enclose the accelerometer positions used with the shaker
position within the same enclosed area.
The colormaps are grids of 6x5 squares, representing each accelerometer position on
the floor. The RT60 result from each measurement position has been evaluated with the
modified Z-score. This score quantifies the deviation in terms of median absolute devia-
tions (MAD) from the median of the results corresponding to the given excitation position.
The black dashed rectangles enclose the accelerometer positions belonging to the shaker
position within the same rectangular enclosure (see accelerometer and shaker configu-
rations in Figure 3.10 in Section 3.1.4).
The colormaps were restricted to mapping only the results from the third-octave bands
with center frequencies corresponding those of the octave bands.
By limiting the in-
vestigation and not mapping all results, the amount of colormaps were reduced from
21 · 30 = 630 to 6 · 30 = 180 colormaps.
The purpose of the investigation were to examine firstly the total statistics; how many
results were outliers (determined as more than 3.5 MADs away from the median). Addi-
tionally, results deviating by 2 MADs were counted to identify larger deviations. Secondly,
it was of interest to determine whether these outliers and larger deviations were primar-
ily located near the excitation positions, outer edges, both, or neither. ”Near excitation
positions” are defined as the four squares that surround each shaker position. As an ex-
ample square B2, B3, C2 and C3 are measurement positions near excitation position 1.
The outer edges are defined as the squares along the perimeter of the floor, and for the
two third-octave bands (2000 Hz and 4000 Hz), the partition line separating the two floor
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements 61
==PAGE:69==
panels is also considered an edge. This is due to the significant deviation in RT60 results,
as determined in Section 3.1.4.
In total 60 measurement positions from each mounting were evaluated, except from; one
faulty measurements for mounting M3.1 and M8.0 each and mounting M1.0, which had
only 30 measurement results. Thus, a total of 1,768 measurements were forming the
basis for the following statistics.
Table 4.5 provides an overview of the total number of measurement positions and their
spatial distribution.

As shown in Table 4.6, the outliers constitute 2-5% of the total amount of measurements
depending on the evaluated third-octave band. Including the larger deviations, they ac-
count for 8-13% of the total amount of measurements.
However, looking at how the combined outliers and larger deviations (MAD > |2|) are
located in the measurement grid in Table 4.7, it can be seen that 76-100% are located
near the shaker and the outer edges. Evaluating only outliers these percentages are 67-
100%. It should be noted that for the 2000 Hz and 4000 Hz third-octave bands, the number
of squares not categorised as ”Near shaker” or ”Outer edges” is limited to 6 positions per
mounting.
In summary, these descriptive statistics reveal that most detected outliers are located near
the excitation positions or outer edges. However, the percentage of outliers relative to the
total number of measurements were low. It should be noted that the latter could indicate
generally good measurements, regardless of the small distances to edges and shaker
positions. Conversely, it could also result from the large percentage of ”near shaker and
outer edges” positions, suggesting that the low percentage of outliers may actually reflect
high precision but low accuracy [27].
4.4.2
Ringing
As previously mentioned, the thesis study by Gotfredsen was conducted simultaneously
with this study. Her simulations indicated that the high loss factors in the lower third-octave
bands obtained with the structural reverberation time method were excessive. Thus, it
became of interest to investigate the validity of the loss factors obtained in this study. The
concept of ringing was studied, which is a concept that can arise when applying filters
with a narrow 3 dB bandwidth to an impulse response with a short reverberation time.
From an article by Finn Jacobsen: ”A Note On Acoustic Decay Measurements” published
in 1987, the effect on the energy decay curve from applying a filter was studied [28]. In the
paper it is written that the effect of ringing is negligibly small if BT60 > 16, where B is the -3
dB bandwidth of the applied filter and T60 is the estimated structural reverberation time.
In Figure 4.13 four boxplots of the structural reverberation times in third-octave bands
for mountings M2.0, M2.1, M7.0 and M7.1 are presented. For conciseness, the ringing
investigations are showed only for these four mountings, as they are considered to be
representative to all measured loss factors.
The four chosen mountings represents the ”loosely placed”- vs ”rigidly fixated” floor, and
the ”loaded”- vs ”unloaded” floor, which where the parameters for which the largest devi-
ations in total loss factor were observed (see Figures 4.1 and 4.2).
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements 63
==PAGE:71==
(a)
(b)
(c)
(d)
Figure 4.13: Boxplots of the BT60 values for each third-octave band for mounting M2.0, M2.1, M7.0 and
M7.1. The BT60 values of 8 and 16 has been marked (black dotted lines) to indicate the threshold at which
the ringing effect can be considered as small and negligible, respectively [28].
In Figure 4.13 BT60 = 16 and BT60 = 8 have been marked in order to show the threshold
at which the ringing can be considered negligible (BT60 = 16), as well as the threshold at
which the ringing can be considered to be small (BT60 = 8). It can be seen that quite many
third-octave bands are affected by ringing, and only in the top three third-octave bands,
3150 Hz to 5000 Hz, the 25th quartile are above the threshold for negligible ringing in all
mountings, Figure 4.13a to 4.13d. However, it is seen that mounting M2 are in general
less affected by ringing than mounting M7, which would be due to longer reverberation
times in the loosely mounted floor.
Nevertheless, the ringing effect would result in overestimated reverberation times, which
would lead to underestimated loss factors. Thus, the effect of ringing, that is seen in
many of the considered third-octave bands wouldn’t explain the excessive loss factors in
the lower third-octave bands seen in Figures 4.1 and 4.2. However, it does show that the
obtained loss factor values presented in the same figures are not precise but rather serve
as indicative estimates. The ringing effect can be seen for all mountings in Appendix 6.
4.4.3
Sources of error from events on site
During measurements several unwanted occurrences was present, which was tried avoided,
but still might have affected the data. The greatest issue was the construction work that
was going on just outside the laboratory building. Low frequency vibration measurements
was greatly influenced by this, which slowed down the measurement process, as mea-
surements were stopped, retaken or corrupted because of low coherence in the low fre-
quency area. Also bumps from cars frequently driving by over metal plates outside was
picked up by the accelerometer on the floor.
64 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:72==
5
Discussion
5.1
Mounting effects on the sound insulation.
The primary focus of this thesis study was to investigate the effect of specific mountings
on the measured sound insulation results of a CLT floor in the laboratory. Therefore,
both loss factor measurements and sound insulation measurements were conducted and
compared in order to study this effect. It was motivated by the absence of a current de-
scription in the ISO standard series 10140 1:5 of a standardized measurement method
and -setup for laboratory measurements of CLT structural elements. The CLT structural
element is similar to the ISO standard concrete reference floor in terms of its solid struc-
ture and to the timber reference floors in terms of its light weight. This leaves room for
interpretation by the test facilitator regarding which standardised measurement procedure
to follow. Therefore, the standard has repeatedly been referenced for comparisons and
to provide perspective on how standard requirements would apply to CLT.
Deviations in measurement results between the mountings
The ISO standard minimum requirement for the loss factor has been frequently referred
to and compared to in the analysis of the obtained loss factor results for the CLT floor
mountings. As has been said, the requirement is there to ”...minimize variation between
measurements in different laboratories” [9].
The requirement doesn’t apply to the CLT floor, as it weigh less than 150 kg/m2, however, it
was seen from the loss factor results that large deviations did occur between the different
mountings and mounting variations. Especially mountings M3 and M7, where the CLT
floor was rigidly fixed in the metal frame, deviated from the other mountings, and likewise
did the mounting variations with and without loading the floor, especially in the mid-lower
third-octave bands.
The deviations in the loss factors were also reflected in the corresponding airborne and
impact sound insulation results.
The normalised impact sound pressure level varied by up to 3 dB, with M1.2 at 84 dB and
M7.0 at 81 dB. Mountings M3 and M7 provided the best results, with normalised impact
sound pressure levels around 82 dB.
The sound reduction index varied by 3–5 dB, ranging from 39 dB for M1.0 to 43.8 dB for
M7.3. The best airborne sound insulation results were, like the impact sound insulation
results, observed for mountings M3 and M7, that gave the highest sound reduction index
values.
This shows that rather large improvements in sound insulation can be obtained by choos-
ing the ”right” mounting.
Looking at the mounting variations, the sound insulation measurement results showed
that loading the floor generally led to higher sound reduction index values across all main
mountings, as expected. Additionally, the loaded floor does mimic the in-situ situation bet-
ter than not loading it, due to the furnishing of a dwelling. Thus, loading the floor improves
sound insulation results and provides a more realistic representation of the floor’s actual
sound insulation performance in-situ. Comparing both impact and airborne sound insu-
lation results for only the loaded floor (variations 1 and 3) across all main mountings this
gives a maximum variation in the sound reduction indexes of 2 dB (43.8 dB for M7.3 and
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements 65
==PAGE:73==
41.8 dB for M1.1), while this variation is 1 dB for the normalised impact sound pressure
level. These variations are much smaller than the variations across all mountings and
mounting variations. Therefore, considering these arguments, a requirement specifying
that CLT floors should be loaded for laboratory sound insulation measurements would be
beneficial. This would help minimize variation between measurements across different
laboratories and yield results that better reflect in-situ conditions.
Practicality aspects
A key aspect of standardized measurement procedures is that they should not be overly
complex nor very time-consuming, ensuring that as many laboratories as possible can
follow them. Loading the floor is a relatively simple and quick mounting implementation,
making it a realistic and simple addition to a standardised measurement method of CLT
floors. On the contrary some of the main mountings investigated in this project are more
complex.
Mounting M7 gave better sound insulation results, and were actually also the only mount-
ing that fulfilled a minimum loss factor requirement that was based on the internal loss
factor of the CLT floor. Additionally, the rigid fixation of the floor in the metal frame pro-
vides a better representation of the actual in-situ situation, where the floor would be rigidly
fixed to the adjacent structural building components with screws. However, this mounting
is very time consuming and complex to perform. The mortar needs to dry for at least three
days, and additionally the entire ”casting in the floor”-process along with breaking up the
mortar for demounting, takes time and are troublesome.
Mounting M3 also revealed slightly better sound insulation results than the remaining
mountings. Similar to M7, this mounting provides a better representation of the in-situ sit-
uation due to the fixation of the floor in the metal frame, but in contrast to M7, this mounting
is not time-consuming - once the long wedges have been acquired. However, the mount-
ing process, which involved hammering down the wooden wedges and ensuring the rigid
fixation of the floor by finding compensations for irregularities in the wooden wedges, the
CLT floor panels, and the metal frame, was still troublesome. Furthermore, comparisons
between mountings M3 and M7 revealed that these irregularities did influence both loss
factor- and sound insulation measurement results. Thereby, this would also indicate the
mounting M3 could potentially lead to different results across various laboratories, test
facilitators, and CLT test elements.
Adjustments to the ISO standard series 10140 1:5?
Although, the two main mountings, M3 and M7, yielded better sound insulation results and
are more realistic representations of the in-situ situation, they are also relatively complex
mountings to perform, thus making them less suitable for inclusion in the ISO standard
series 10140. Including mounting requirements like M3 or M7 in the ISO standard could
lead to more ”work-arounds” across the different labs and test facilitators as these mount-
ings are too comprehensive. Thus, an attempt to minimize variations between different
laboratories could ironically contribute to the same amount of variation, or even more,
between laboratories.
Although mountings M3 and M7 may be too complex and time-consuming to implement as
standardized laboratory measurement requirements for CLT floors, it is problematic that
they give better sound insulation results. It is problematic in the sense that if a relevant
party is interested, they could simply purchase these mountings. The problem could be
mitigated by implementing a loading requirement for CLT floors to the ISO standard, as
the variation across these mountings were seen to be low.
66 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:74==
Alternatively, it could be considered to implement a minimum loss factor requirement for
CLT floors, likewise the standing requirement for heavyweight floors. By using the internal
loss factor constant of ηint = 0.02 in a possible CLT floor minimum loss factor requirement,
only mounting M7 would succeed to meet this requirement. Thereby, indirectly laborato-
ries would have no other alternative than to perform mounting M7, and thereby variations
in sound insulation measurement results across different laboratories would be greatly
minimized. However, such a requirement is just as comprehensive as an explicit M7 or
M3 mounting requirement, and similarly such a requirement could possibly contribute to
a larger variation in measurement results between labs.
5.2
Structural reverberation time measurements
Various analyses laid ground for the final measurement setup chosen for the structural
reverberation time measurements conducted in this study. Therefore, this also gave rise
to several sources of error.
Measurement setup - Location of measurement positions
Early in the measurement period, it was determined to have the measurement positions
arranged in a measurement grid. However, many of the measurement positions in the
grid did not meet the distancing requirements specified in ISO standard 10848-1, section
7.3.3. Additionally, it was doubtful whether the number of shaker positions used in this
project met with the required three. Four shaker positions were used for each mounting
but only two positions were used on each floor panel. However, due to the reduced space
on each floor panel, it also wouldn’t be realistic to have three excitation positions on each
panel.
Although, neither the number of excitation positions nor the distancing requirements were
fully met, the number of measurement positions by far exceeded the requirement of three
measurement positions per excitation position. Initially, this was considered as compen-
sation for the two unmet requirements, but was subsequently investigated in terms of the
location of outliers.
This investigation showed that out of a total of 1,768 measurement positions, as much
as 77% were measurement positions near edges or near the excitation point in the third
octave bands of 125 Hz, 250 Hz, 500 Hz and 1000 Hz. Only 2-4% of all measurement
positions in the same third-octave bands were outliers, although 8-12% were deviating
with more than |2| MADs. This indicated that measuring near edges or excitation positions
would rarely result in faulty measurements. However, one might still observe a larger
spread in the obtained measurement results, when measuring at these positions.
Influence of the floor panel partition line on high frequency vibrations
In the third-octave bands of 2000 Hz and 4000 Hz 87% of the positions were categorized
as ”near edge” and ”near shaker” positions. The increase in positions was due to the floor
panel partition line being considered as an edge in these higher third-octave bands.
The consideration was based on preliminary structural reverberation time analysis of
mounting M1.0, investigating if the RT60 results obtained from the accelerometer posi-
tions on the same floor partition as the excitation position and RT60 results obtained
from the accelerometer positions on the opposite floor partition as the excitation posi-
tion, were results from the same normal distribution with a significance level of 5%. The
analysis revealed a significant difference in the third-octave bands of 50 Hz, 315 Hz, 630
Hz, 800 Hz, 1000 Hz, 1250 Hz, 1600 Hz, 2000 Hz, 2500 Hz, 3150 Hz, 4000 Hz, 5000 Hz.
This analysis lead to the conclusion that the transfer of vibrational energy between the
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements 67
==PAGE:75==
two floor panels was somehow disrupted in these frequency bands. Additionally boxplots
of the results revealed that especially in the highest third-octave bands from 2000 Hz and
up, the results were very different between the two measurement and excitation position
configurations.
The consideration of floor panel partition being an edge, left very few measurement po-
sitions uncategorised. However, only 3–5% of the ”near edge” and ”near shaker” mea-
surement results were outliers, while 10–13% deviated by more than |2| MADs, leading to
a similar observation as for the other third-octave bands: ”near edge” and ”near shaker”
measurement positions rarely gives wrong results, however result in a larger spread in
the obtained measurement results.
Accuracy or preciseness?
Although the above statistics suggests that meeting the distancing requirements from the
ISO standard 10848-1 is not essential for obtaining reliable measurement results, there
remains the possibility that, the obtained results reveal high preciseness but low accuracy.
This is due to the high percentage of the total amount of measurement positions being
categorised as ”near shaker and outer edges”. Thus, the conducted data for the structural
reverberation time measurements did contain few outliers, however the whole dataset
could be faulty and misleading.
Nevertheless, the theoretical total loss factor of the CLT floor did align with the measured
loss factors for the mounting variations without loading (variation 0 and 2). This would
indicate that the measured loss factors weren’t inaccurate. Thus, despite the numerous
measurement positions violating the ISO standard distance requirements and despite the
ringing that influenced many of the third-octave bands of consideration, the loss factor
values obtained from the measurements may still be reasonably accurate.
Practicality aspects
The structural reverberation time measurements were highly time-consuming, requiring
approximately two hours to complete a full mounting measurement with 15 measurement
positions for each of four excitation positions. The two main time-consuming parts were;
• only measuring at one measurement position at a time due to PULSE only permitting
a single input channel during measurement and,
• aligning the shaker for each new position ensuring the impact force to be as vertical
as possible on the floor.
The first issue could be mitigated by reducing the number of measurement positions. As
observed in the measurement position analysis, the results did not deviate much from one
another, suggesting that fewer positions could be sufficient. Conversely, a larger number
of measurements is an advantages as it contributes to greater stability in the research.
The first issue could have also been mitigated by using DIRAC instead of PULSE, as
DIRAC allows for multiple simultaneous measurements. However, the trade-off is the
inability to view coherence graphs during measurements, which could potentially result in
a greater number of faulty measurements. The alignment of the shaker was the weakest
point in the measurement setup used in this project, as it relied on the precision of manual
work. Therefore, the coherence graphs were of great importance for this measurement
setup and multiple times during measurements the equipment were in fact adjusted due
to bad coherence.
68 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:76==
5.3
Theoretical results of transmission loss and normalized
impact sound pressure level
Although the theoretical results obtained using SEA initially appeared to deviate signifi-
cantly from the measured results, a closer examination revealed that some of the theo-
retical predictions actually aligned with the measured data. This regards the curve shape
of the transmission loss that apart from the additional mode observed at 315 Hz, the dip
around 2000-3150 Hz caused by the first thickness resonance in the plate and the gen-
eral upward shift in peaks aligned well with the measured data. The upward shifted peaks
was likely due to inaccuracies in identifying the theoretically calculated modes in the plate,
fnm. Incorrect mode identification is entirely plausible, as the theory does not account for
all the unknown parameters introduced by the specific floor mounting in practice. The
mounting will influence the normal modes in the plate, and this was also clearly observed
for mounting M8 with VIKAFOAM. Here, the mounting of the floor on the elastic material
caused a shift in the modes in the plate, resulting in differences compared to the other
mountings.
Regarding the normalized impact sound pressure level, a similar upward shift in modes
was observed (visible as dips in the results). However, the shape of this curve did not
align well with the measured Ln. Nevertheless, when using the impact force obtained
from Gotfredsen, the shape of the theoretical results above 500 Hz was similar to that
of the measured results. This reflects the change in frequency excitation induced by the
dropping hammers in the tapping machine. Due to the softness of the wood, the contact
time between hammer and floor is elongated, wherefore frequency excitation will bend off
earlier than if the floor was hard and stiff like concrete.
There are several reasons why the theoretical results did not align precisely with the mea-
sured results. However, this discrepancy fundamentally arises from the nature of theoret-
ical models as simplifications of the real world. Numerous assumptions inherent to SEA
were not satisfied across a broad spectrum of evaluated third-octave bands. Furthermore,
even in the higher frequency range, where modal density and modal overlap began to in-
crease, thus satisfying the general assumptions for SEA, the theoretical results still didn’t
align with the measured data. Therefore, this form of analysis is mainly useful for under-
standing the behaviours in the structure (or system) rather than predicting its actual sound
insulation properties.
5.4
Repeatability measurements
A deeper analysis of the obtained results from the repeatability investigations of sound
pressure level measurements in Room 904, was lacking in this report, due to time limi-
tations. However, a preliminary analysis of the means, standard deviations, and double-
sided expanded uncertainty was made from the four types of measurement configurations;
Source in Room 904 or 004 with either the same or anew microphone positions between
measurements.
Spread in sound pressure level measurement results
The main focus of these investigations were to examine the uncertainty related to mea-
surements in the lower third-octave bands; 50 Hz, 63 Hz and 80 Hz. It was motivated
by the reasoning that it is not a requirement in the ISO standard to report measurement
results in these lower third-octave bands, resulting in some labs reporting these results
and some don’t. However, it is the low frequencies that especially are problematic to
treat in the acoustic design of buildings, and especially for lightweight structural building
materials the frequencies below 100 Hz would be problematic.
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements 69
==PAGE:77==
The results revealed a relatively large spread of 5-10 dB in the standard deviation intervals
in the measurement results in the low third-octave bands below 100 Hz, whereas the
spread was much narrower in the third-octave bands from 100 Hz and up, particularly
narrow from around 1000 Hz. This outcome was expected, due to the non-diffuseness of
the sound field in these low third-octave bands. Theoretically, the sound field in the room
can be considered diffuse above the Schröder frequency, which is approximately 250 Hz
in Room 904.
The results make complete sense of ISO standard requirement regarding the measure-
ment range. A deviation of 5-10 dB is a large deviation and reporting these values would
reflect inaccurate sound insulation properties of the test element.
It wasn’t specifically investigated if the increasing number of microphone positions in
Room 904 would contribute to more accurate measurement results. However, from the
plots made, it could be seen that the standard deviation for all three microphone cate-
gories were quite similar. Therefore, without doing any inferential statistical tests of the
data, the expectation to the outcome of those would be that increasing microphone posi-
tions would not contribute to a decreased uncertainty in the third-octave bands below 100
Hz.
70 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:78==
6
Conclusion
In conclusion this study shows that better sound insulation results of CLT floors can be
obtained in laboratory sound insulation measurements by rigidly fixating the floor in the
test opening - i.e. mounting M3 and M7. Additionally a rigid fixation of the floor pro-
vides with a better representation of the in-situ situation. However, these mountings are
comprehensive, time-consuming and impractical to perform which would argue against
them being implemented as mounting requirements in the ISO standard series 10140 1-5.
Conversely, loading the CLT floor with 25 kg/m2 is a relatively simple and quick mounting
to perform making it realistic and easy to implement in the ISO standard. Furthermore
loading the floor would also provide with a better representation of the in-situ situation,
similar to the rigid fixation of the floor. The sound insulation measurement results across
mountings with loaded floors revealed smaller variations than for the results across all
mountings and mounting variations. This leads to the perception that by implementing a
loading requirement to the ISO standard for laboratory sound insulation measurements
on CLT floors, the variation in measurement results across laboratories worldwide would
be minimized.
The study and comparison of theoretical results with measured results validated the fact
that theory serves as a simplified representation of reality. While theoretical results can
assist in interpreting measured results, they cannot precisely predict neither the sound
reduction index nor the normalized impact sound pressure level of a structure in real-
world scenarios. Moreover, the theoretical model lacks accuracy for thick plates, as it
does not account for resonances in the thickness direction.
Furthermore, this study revealed a mechanical disruption in the transfer of structural high-
frequency vibrations between the panels in CLT floors composed of two panels. Thus, this
configuration, that is commonly used in laboratories, necessitates careful consideration
of this mechanical disruption, as it may influence the measurement results.
It is challenging to draw any conclusions based on the limited analysis performed on the
repeatability measurement results. However, tendencies and indications from the mea-
surement results can be reported. Firstly measurement results obtained from standing
microphone positions revealed generally lower sound pressure levels in the third-octave
bands of 2500 Hz and up. Secondly, the measurement results support the reporting re-
quirements for the measurement range as specified by the ISO standard, and the reason
for not extending it down to 50 Hz. The measurement results revealed relatively large un-
certainties in the third-octave bands below 100 Hz, wherefore repeatability and reliability
of single measurement results would be doubtful.
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements 71

Table 2: Schematic overview of all reverberation time adjustment measurements. WB is short for wooden
box as earlier established in Table 1.
The considerations behind these absorber designs and combinations, were the type of
the absorber, the size of the absorber and the placement of the absorber.
A porous absorber absorbs more energy in the high-frequency range due to the small
pores in the material. The small pores within the material allows for shorter wavelengths
to penetrate whereby acoustic energy dissipates into thermal and viscous losses. How-
ever, the thicker the absorber, the more low frequency sound is absorbed. The membrane
absorber is typically more effective at narrow and low frequencies below 500 Hz. It makes
use of the concept of acoustic resonance with a coupled mass, i.e. the membrane, and
spring, i.e. the absorbing material. By increasing the surface mass of the membrane or
by increasing the cavity depth the target frequency area can be shifted downwards. The
Helmholtz resonator design was considered when removing the front side of the 600 mm
absorber with box, aiming for effective absorption around the 50 Hz third-octave band.
However, measurements and subsequent calculations proved the dimensions of this ab-
sorber not to be large enough to reach this target frequency. Therefore, the boxed 600
mm absorber was replaced with an even deeper porous absorber, to be able to reach a
wide frequency spectrum and also reach the low third-octave bands of 50 Hz and 63 Hz
[29].
The positioning of the absorbers in corners vs. alongside a wall was to increase the
possibility of reaching the low frequency third-octave bands. The increasing possibility is
due to greater amount of acoustic energy that is present in corners in respect to walls,
theoretically explained by the Waterhouse effect [30].

114 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements

Appendix D
Measurement log from sound insulation measurements
The six tapping machine positions as well as the loading weight positions can be seen in
Figure 26.
Figure 26: Tapping machine- and weights positions mapped on the floor. North of the picture is toward Room
003 and South of the picture is toward the workshop hall, i.e. floor partition is horizontal. The long lines with
three perpendicular smaller lines are the tapping machine positions and the rectangles with crosses are the
weights positions. The tapping machine positions are labelled with number 1-6. The measure unit is in mm.
The tapping machine positions were the same throughout all sound insulation measure-
ments and the positioning of the weights of 25 kg were the same throughout all measure-
ments on mounting variations with loading. Both tapping machine positions and weights
positions followed the standard positioning for laboratory sound insulation measurements
used in FORCE Technology.
In the following, all measurement logbooks, written in Danish, for each sound insulation
measurement can be seen. The logbook contains;
• date of measurement conduction,
• a description of the mounting and mounting variation,
• the prior- and post-measurement calibrations,
• the file number on the SLM,
• type of measurement conducted,
• number of measurements for each type of measurement,
• traverse speeds of the rotating boom,
• information on reused measurements,
• information on source and receiver room,
Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements 115
==PAGE:123==
• weighted sound reduction index and spectrum adaption terms C and Ctr obtained
from the SLM,
• the apparent room temperature, relative humidity and pressure in the rooms,
• and closing notes for the measurement(s) conducted on the current day.
The mounting(s) covered in each individual logbook is(are) named in the caption to each
sub-figure with the mounting labelling code used in this thesis project.
(a) M1.0
(b) M1.1
(c) M1.3
(d) M1.2

(f) M2.1 and M2.3

Figure 27: Measurement logbooks of all sound insulation measurements conducted. The logbooks are in
Danish.
In Figure 27b, 27c and 27d it can be seen that the weighted sound reduction index, Rw,
is around 60 dB. The large value of Rw is due to the microphone in Room 904 mistakenly
being gained by 20 dB. The 20 dB was subtracted after measurements from the measured
background noise, B2, in Room 904 and the level difference values, L1L2, in all third-
octave bands.
The impact sound insulation measurements were, as mentioned in Section 3, measured
with an airborne sound insulation measurement template on the SLM, in order to be able to
correct for airborne sound transmission. The airborne sound transmission corrections as
well as the background noise corrections were made in accordance with the ISO standard
10140 3:4.
In Table 7, 8 and 9 all sound reduction indexes, R, and the weighted sound reduction
index, Rw can be seen along with the spectral adaption terms c and ctr for every mounting
measured. Furthermore the third-octave bands to which corrections have been made are
also marked with colours.

Investigation of ringing from applied filter
This section includes the ringing investigations for every mounting and mounting varia-
tion. If BT60 is above 16, the ringing effect from applying the third-octave band filters is
negligible.
Mounting M1

The longitudinal wave speed in the thickness direction of the CLT floor has been obtained
from the study: ”Elastic wave modes for the assessment of structural timber: Ultrasonic
echo for building elements and guided waves for pole and pile structures” from 2014 [24].
It has been determined from the mean of the values given in Figure 2 in the referred to
study. The figure is presented in this appendix in Figure 48
166 Development of laboratory sound insulation measurement methods of cross-laminated timber elements
==PAGE:174==
Figure 48: Figure 2 from the study: ”Elastic wave modes for the assessment of structural timber: Ultrasonic
echo for building elements and guided waves for pole and pile structures” from 2014 [24].
The mean value is determined from a rough estimate of the six wave speeds in the thick-
ness direction depicted in Figure 48 and is
clz = 1200[m/s] + 1400[m/s] + 1600[m/s] + 1800[m/s] + 1700[m/s] + 1600[m/s]
